## Chapter: Exponential growth & decay

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Exponential Growth and Decay, a fundamental concept in Algebra 1. This chapter will delve into the fascinating world of exponential functions and their real-world applications, particularly in modeling growth and decay phenomena.

Exponential growth and decay are mathematical concepts that describe the way quantities grow or decrease over time. They are ubiquitous in various fields, including physics, biology, economics, and many more. Understanding these concepts is crucial for anyone who wants to master Algebra and apply it in real-world scenarios.

In this chapter, we will start by introducing the basic form of an exponential function, which is typically written as $f(x) = a \cdot b^{x}$, where $a$ and $b$ are constants. We will then explore the properties of these functions, focusing on how they can represent either growth or decay depending on the value of $b$.

We will also discuss the concept of the base of an exponential function, which is the number $b$ in the function $f(x) = a \cdot b^{x}$. The base plays a crucial role in determining whether the function represents growth or decay. For instance, if $b > 1$, the function represents exponential growth, while if $0 < b < 1$, it represents exponential decay.

Finally, we will apply these concepts to solve real-world problems involving exponential growth and decay. This will not only solidify your understanding of these concepts but also demonstrate their practical relevance.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of exponential growth and decay, and you will be able to apply these concepts to solve a wide range of problems. So, let's dive in and start exploring the fascinating world of exponential functions!

## Chapter: Exponential growth & decay

### Section: Exponential vs. linear growth

In this section, we will compare and contrast exponential growth with linear growth. Understanding the differences between these two types of growth is crucial for understanding the behavior of different mathematical models and their real-world applications.

#### Linear Growth

Linear growth is the simplest type of growth. It occurs when a quantity increases by a fixed amount over equal intervals of time. This is represented by a straight line when graphed. The general form of a linear function is $f(x) = mx + c$, where $m$ is the slope of the line (representing the rate of growth) and $c$ is the y-intercept (representing the initial value).

#### Exponential Growth

As we have already discussed, exponential growth occurs when a quantity increases by a fixed percentage over equal intervals of time. This is represented by a curve when graphed. The general form of an exponential function is $f(x) = a \cdot b^{x}$, where $a$ is the initial value and $b$ is the growth factor.

#### Comparing exponential and linear growth

The key difference between linear and exponential growth lies in the rate of growth. In linear growth, the rate of growth is constant, meaning that the quantity increases by the same amount over each interval of time. In contrast, in exponential growth, the rate of growth is proportional to the current quantity, meaning that the quantity increases by a larger amount over each interval of time as the quantity itself gets larger.

This difference has significant implications. For example, if a population of bacteria is growing exponentially, it will increase much more rapidly over time than if it were growing linearly. Similarly, an investment that grows exponentially (such as compound interest) will yield much greater returns over time than an investment that grows linearly.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into these concepts and explore how to calculate and interpret exponential and linear growth rates. We will also look at some real-world examples to illustrate these concepts. So, stay tuned!

#### Understanding exponential growth

Exponential growth can be a challenging concept to grasp, but it is a fundamental aspect of many natural and financial phenomena. To better understand exponential growth, let's break it down.

##### The Exponential Function

The exponential function, $f(x) = a \cdot b^{x}$, is the mathematical representation of exponential growth. Here, $a$ is the initial value, $b$ is the growth factor, and $x$ is the time interval. 

The growth factor, $b$, is particularly important. If $b > 1$, the function represents exponential growth, and if $0 < b < 1$, it represents exponential decay. 

##### Exponential Growth in Real-World Scenarios

Exponential growth is often seen in real-world scenarios. For example, consider a population of bacteria. If each bacterium divides into two every hour, the population will double every hour. This is exponential growth, as the rate of growth is proportional to the current population. 

Similarly, compound interest is another example of exponential growth. If you invest a certain amount of money in a bank account that compounds interest annually, the amount of money in the account will grow exponentially over time. This is because the interest is calculated on the initial principal and the accumulated interest of previous periods.

##### Calculating Exponential Growth

To calculate exponential growth, we use the formula $f(x) = a \cdot b^{x}$. 

For example, if we start with a population of 100 bacteria and the population doubles every hour, we can represent this with the function $f(x) = 100 \cdot 2^{x}$, where $x$ is the number of hours. 

After 1 hour, the population would be $f(1) = 100 \cdot 2^{1} = 200$. After 2 hours, the population would be $f(2) = 100 \cdot 2^{2} = 400$, and so on. 

As you can see, the population grows rapidly due to the exponential nature of the growth.

In the next section, we will explore exponential decay and how it contrasts with exponential growth.

### Exponential vs. Linear Growth

Before we delve into the real-world applications of exponential growth, it's important to understand how it differs from linear growth. 

#### Linear Growth

Linear growth is represented by the function $f(x) = mx + c$, where $m$ is the slope of the line and $c$ is the y-intercept. In the context of linear growth, $m$ represents the constant rate of change, and $x$ is the time interval. 

For example, if you save $100$ every month, the total amount of money you have saved over time would be represented by a linear function. After 1 month, you would have $100$, after 2 months, you would have $200$, and so on. The amount of money you save grows at a constant rate.

#### Comparing Exponential and Linear Growth

The key difference between exponential and linear growth lies in the rate of change. In linear growth, the rate of change is constant. This means that the amount by which the value increases is the same for each step in time. 

In contrast, in exponential growth, the rate of change is proportional to the current value. This means that the amount by which the value increases gets larger with each step in time. 

To illustrate this, let's compare the linear function $f(x) = 100x$ (representing saving $100$ every month) with the exponential function $g(x) = 100 \cdot 2^{x}$ (representing a population of bacteria that doubles every hour). 

After 1 hour (or month), both functions give us $200$. However, after 2 hours, the linear function gives us $400$, while the exponential function gives us $800$. After 3 hours, the linear function gives us $600$, while the exponential function gives us $1600$. As you can see, the exponential function grows much more rapidly than the linear function.

### Real-world applications of exponential growth

Exponential growth is not just a mathematical concept; it has numerous real-world applications. We've already discussed how it applies to population growth and compound interest, but let's explore a few more examples.

#### Technological Advancements

One of the most famous examples of exponential growth is Moore's Law. Named after Gordon Moore, the co-founder of Intel, it states that the number of transistors on a microchip doubles approximately every two years. This has led to an exponential increase in computing power over the past few decades.

#### Spread of Diseases

Exponential growth also models the spread of diseases. When a disease is contagious, each infected person can infect multiple others. This can lead to an exponential increase in the number of infected individuals, especially if no measures are taken to control the spread.

#### Economic Growth

In economics, exponential growth is often used to model the growth of investments or the economy as a whole. For example, if an economy grows at a constant percentage rate, it is experiencing exponential growth.

In the next section, we will explore exponential decay and how it contrasts with exponential growth.

### Section: Exponential expressions

Exponential expressions are mathematical expressions that involve exponents. An exponent refers to the number of times a number is multiplied by itself. For example, in the expression $2^3$, 2 is the base and 3 is the exponent, meaning that 2 is multiplied by itself 3 times ($2*2*2$) to give 8.

#### Writing exponential expressions

Writing exponential expressions involves identifying the base and the exponent. The base is the number that is being multiplied, and the exponent is the number of times the base is being multiplied by itself. 

For example, in the expression $5^4$, 5 is the base and 4 is the exponent. This means that 5 is multiplied by itself 4 times ($5*5*5*5$) to give 625.

In the context of exponential growth, the base represents the initial amount and the exponent represents the rate of growth. For example, if a population of bacteria doubles every hour, the exponential expression representing this growth could be $2^x$, where 2 is the base (the initial population) and $x$ is the exponent (the number of hours).

#### Simplifying exponential expressions

Simplifying exponential expressions involves applying the laws of exponents. Here are some of the basic laws of exponents:

1. Product of powers rule: $a^m * a^n = a^{m+n}$
2. Quotient of powers rule: $a^m / a^n = a^{m-n}$
3. Power of a power rule: $(a^m)^n = a^{mn}$
4. Zero exponent rule: $a^0 = 1$
5. Negative exponent rule: $a^{-n} = 1/a^n$

Using these rules, we can simplify complex exponential expressions. For example, $(2^3)^2$ can be simplified to $2^6$ using the power of a power rule, which equals 64.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of exponential decay, which is the opposite of exponential growth. We will learn how to write and simplify exponential expressions representing decay, and explore real-world applications of this concept.

### Section: Exponential decay

Exponential decay is a specific type of decrease where a quantity decreases at a rate proportional to its current value. This is the opposite of exponential growth, where a quantity increases at a rate proportional to its current value.

#### Writing exponential decay expressions

Writing exponential decay expressions involves identifying the base and the exponent, similar to writing exponential growth expressions. However, in the context of exponential decay, the base represents the initial amount and the exponent represents the rate of decay.

For example, if a radioactive substance decays to half its original amount every year, the exponential expression representing this decay could be $0.5^x$, where 0.5 is the base (the initial amount) and $x$ is the exponent (the number of years).

#### Simplifying exponential decay expressions

Simplifying exponential decay expressions also involves applying the laws of exponents. For example, $(0.5^2)^3$ can be simplified to $0.5^6$ using the power of a power rule, which equals approximately 0.015625.

### Subsection: Real-world applications of exponential decay

Exponential decay is a concept that is widely applicable in various real-world scenarios. It is used in physics to model the decay of radioactive substances, in finance to calculate depreciation, in biology to model population decline, and in many other fields.

For example, in finance, if a car worth $20,000 depreciates by 10% each year, the value of the car after $x$ years can be represented by the exponential decay expression $20000*(0.9)^x$.

In the next section, we will explore more complex exponential expressions and learn how to simplify them using the laws of exponents. We will also look at more real-world applications of exponential growth and decay.

### Section: Exponential expressions

Exponential expressions are mathematical expressions that involve exponents. They are used to represent quantities that grow or decay at a rate proportional to their current value. In this section, we will delve deeper into exponential expressions, exploring how to write, simplify, and use them to model real-world situations.

#### Writing exponential expressions

Writing exponential expressions involves identifying the base and the exponent. The base represents the initial amount, while the exponent represents the rate of growth or decay.

For example, if a population of bacteria doubles every hour, the exponential expression representing this growth could be $2^x$, where 2 is the base (the initial amount) and $x$ is the exponent (the number of hours).

#### Simplifying exponential expressions

Simplifying exponential expressions involves applying the laws of exponents. For instance, $(2^3)^4$ can be simplified to $2^{3*4}$ or $2^{12}$ using the power of a power rule.

### Subsection: Using exponential expressions to model situations

Exponential expressions can be used to model a variety of real-world situations, from population growth to radioactive decay. 

For example, let's consider a situation where a population of rabbits doubles every month. If we start with 2 rabbits, the population after $x$ months can be represented by the exponential expression $2^x$.

In another example, if a car worth $30,000 depreciates by 15% each year, the value of the car after $x$ years can be represented by the exponential decay expression $30000*(0.85)^x$.

Understanding how to write and simplify exponential expressions is crucial for modeling these types of situations. In the next section, we will explore more complex exponential expressions and learn how to solve them using the laws of exponents.

## Chapter: Exponential growth & decay

### Section: Graphs of exponential growth

In the previous section, we learned about exponential expressions and how they can be used to model real-world situations. Now, we will explore how to graph these expressions to visualize exponential growth.

#### Understanding graphs of exponential functions

An exponential function is a function of the form $f(x) = a*b^x$, where $a$ and $b$ are constants, $b > 0$, $b \neq 1$, and $x$ is any real number. The graph of an exponential function is a curve that either increases or decreases rapidly depending on the value of $b$.

##### Graphing exponential growth

When $b > 1$, the function represents exponential growth. The graph of an exponential growth function rises from left to right. 

For example, consider the function $f(x) = 2^x$. This function represents exponential growth because the base, 2, is greater than 1. The graph of this function starts near 0 for negative $x$ values, crosses the y-axis at $y = 1$ (since any number to the power of 0 is 1), and then rises rapidly for positive $x$ values.

Here's how you can graph the function $f(x) = 2^x$:

1. Create a table of values for $x$ and $f(x)$.
2. Plot these values on a graph.
3. Connect the points to form a smooth curve.

| $x$ | $f(x)$ |
| --- | ------ |
| -2  | 0.25   |
| -1  | 0.5    |
| 0   | 1      |
| 1   | 2      |
| 2   | 4      |

The graph of $f(x) = 2^x$ will look like a curve that starts near 0 for negative $x$ values, crosses the y-axis at $y = 1$, and then rises rapidly for positive $x$ values.

Understanding the graph of an exponential function is crucial for interpreting the behavior of the function and predicting future values. In the next section, we will explore the graphs of exponential decay functions.

#### Graphing exponential growth functions

In this section, we will delve deeper into the process of graphing exponential growth functions. We will use the function $f(x) = 2^x$ as our example throughout this section.

##### Step 1: Create a table of values

The first step in graphing an exponential function is to create a table of values. This table will include a range of $x$ values and their corresponding $f(x)$ values. For the function $f(x) = 2^x$, the table might look like this:

| $x$ | $f(x)$ |
| --- | ------ |
| -3  | 0.125  |
| -2  | 0.25   |
| -1  | 0.5    |
| 0   | 1      |
| 1   | 2      |
| 2   | 4      |
| 3   | 8      |

##### Step 2: Plot the points

Next, plot the points from your table on a graph. The $x$ values will be on the horizontal axis, and the $f(x)$ values will be on the vertical axis. For the function $f(x) = 2^x$, the points will start near 0 for negative $x$ values, cross the y-axis at $y = 1$, and then rise rapidly for positive $x$ values.

##### Step 3: Draw the curve

Finally, connect the points with a smooth curve. The curve should start near 0 for negative $x$ values, cross the y-axis at $y = 1$, and then rise rapidly for positive $x$ values. This curve represents the graph of the function $f(x) = 2^x$.

##### Understanding the graph

The graph of an exponential growth function has a few key characteristics:

- The graph crosses the y-axis at $y = 1$. This is because any number to the power of 0 is 1.
- The graph rises rapidly for positive $x$ values. This represents the exponential growth of the function.
- The graph approaches 0 for negative $x$ values but never actually reaches 0. This is because a positive number to the power of a negative number is a positive fraction, which approaches 0 as the negative number becomes larger in magnitude.

Understanding these characteristics can help you interpret the graph and predict the behavior of the function. In the next section, we will explore how to graph exponential decay functions.

### Section: Interpreting graphs of exponential growth

Understanding the graph of an exponential growth function is crucial in interpreting the behavior of the function. In this section, we will discuss how to interpret the graph of an exponential growth function using the function $f(x) = 2^x$ as an example.

#### Subsection: Identifying key points on the graph

The graph of an exponential growth function has several key points that can help us understand the behavior of the function. For the function $f(x) = 2^x$, these key points include:

- The y-intercept: The graph crosses the y-axis at $y = 1$. This is because any number to the power of 0 is 1. This point is often referred to as the initial value of the function.

- The horizontal asymptote: The graph approaches 0 for negative $x$ values but never actually reaches 0. This is because a positive number to the power of a negative number is a positive fraction, which approaches 0 as the negative number becomes larger in magnitude. This line $y = 0$ is called the horizontal asymptote of the function.

- The growth rate: The graph rises rapidly for positive $x$ values. This represents the exponential growth of the function. The rate at which the function grows is determined by the base of the exponent. In this case, the base is 2, so the function doubles for each unit increase in $x$.

#### Subsection: Analyzing the behavior of the function

Once we have identified the key points on the graph, we can analyze the behavior of the function. For the function $f(x) = 2^x$, we can make the following observations:

- For negative $x$ values, the function approaches 0 but never reaches it. This means that the function has a lower bound of 0.

- For positive $x$ values, the function grows rapidly. This means that the function has no upper bound.

- The function is always increasing. This means that the function is always growing, never decreasing.

- The function is not linear. This means that the rate of growth is not constant, but instead increases as $x$ increases.

Understanding these behaviors can help us predict the future behavior of the function and interpret real-world situations modeled by the function. In the next section, we will explore how to graph and interpret exponential decay functions.

### Section: Exponential vs. linear growth over time

In the previous sections, we have explored the characteristics and behavior of exponential growth. Now, let's compare exponential growth with linear growth over time. This comparison will help us understand the fundamental differences between these two types of growth and their implications in various real-world scenarios.

#### Subsection: Understanding linear growth

Before we delve into the comparison, let's briefly review what linear growth is. A linear function has the form $f(x) = mx + b$, where $m$ is the slope of the line and $b$ is the y-intercept. The slope $m$ represents the constant rate of change of the function. This means that for every unit increase in $x$, the function $f(x)$ increases by $m$ units. 

For example, if we have a linear function $f(x) = 2x + 3$, the slope is 2, which means the function increases by 2 units for every unit increase in $x$. The y-intercept is 3, which is the value of the function when $x = 0$.

#### Subsection: Comparing exponential and linear growth over time

Now that we have a basic understanding of both exponential and linear growth, let's compare them. 

- Rate of growth: The most significant difference between exponential and linear growth is the rate at which they grow. Linear growth increases at a constant rate, while exponential growth increases at a rate that itself increases over time. In other words, in linear growth, the function increases by the same amount for each unit increase in $x$. In contrast, in exponential growth, the function doubles (or triples, or quadruples, etc., depending on the base of the exponent) for each unit increase in $x$.

- Graphical representation: The graph of a linear function is a straight line, while the graph of an exponential function is a curve that becomes steeper as $x$ increases. 

- Long-term behavior: Over a short period, linear and exponential growth might seem similar. However, over a long period, exponential growth will always surpass linear growth, no matter the initial conditions or the rate of linear growth. This is because the rate of exponential growth increases over time, while the rate of linear growth remains constant.

In the next section, we will explore some real-world examples of exponential and linear growth to further illustrate these concepts.

outpaces linear growth by a significant margin. This is because the rate of increase in exponential growth is proportional to the current value, leading to a rapid increase over time.

#### Subsection: Modeling with exponential and linear functions

Both exponential and linear functions are powerful tools for modeling real-world scenarios. However, the choice between using an exponential or a linear function depends on the nature of the phenomenon being modeled.

##### Linear models

Linear functions are used when the rate of change is constant. For example, if you save $100 every month, the total amount of money saved over time can be modeled by a linear function. Here, the slope $m$ would be 100 (the amount saved each month), and the y-intercept $b$ would be the initial amount of money saved.

##### Exponential models

On the other hand, exponential functions are used when the rate of change is not constant but is proportional to the current value. For instance, the growth of a population can often be modeled by an exponential function. If a population doubles every year, the number of individuals in the population at any given time can be represented by an exponential function. Here, the base of the exponent would be 2 (representing the doubling each year), and the exponent would be the number of years.

In conclusion, understanding the differences between exponential and linear growth is crucial in algebra. It allows us to choose the appropriate model for a given situation, which in turn enables us to make accurate predictions and informed decisions. ### Section: Exponential vs. linear growth over time

In this section, we will compare and contrast exponential and linear growth over time. Understanding the differences between these two types of growth is crucial for interpreting and creating mathematical models in algebra.

#### Subsection: Interpreting exponential and linear models

##### Linear Growth

Linear growth is characterized by a constant rate of change. This means that the amount by which a quantity increases (or decreases) remains the same over time. In a graph, linear growth is represented by a straight line. The slope of this line, denoted by $m$, represents the constant rate of change. 

For example, if you are saving $100 every month, the total amount of money saved over time can be represented by a straight line with a slope of 100. This is because the amount of money saved increases by the same amount ($100) each month.

##### Exponential Growth

Exponential growth, on the other hand, is characterized by a rate of change that is proportional to the current value. This means that the amount by which a quantity increases (or decreases) gets larger (or smaller) over time. In a graph, exponential growth is represented by a curve that becomes steeper as time progresses.

For instance, if a population of bacteria doubles every hour, the number of bacteria at any given time can be represented by an exponential function. Here, the base of the exponent would be 2 (representing the doubling each hour), and the exponent would be the number of hours.

##### Comparing Linear and Exponential Growth

When comparing linear and exponential growth, it's important to note that while linear growth adds a constant amount over each interval of time, exponential growth multiplies by a constant factor over each interval of time. 

This leads to a key difference between the two: while linear growth is steady and predictable, exponential growth can quickly become very large. This is why exponential growth is often associated with phenomena such as population growth or the spread of diseases, where small initial changes can lead to large effects over time.

In the next section, we will explore exponential decay, which is a specific type of exponential growth where the quantity decreases over time.

### Section: Exponential growth & decay

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of exponential growth. Now, let's delve into the concept of exponential decay, which is just as important and interesting.

#### Subsection: Understanding exponential decay

Exponential decay is a specific form of exponential change where the quantity decreases over time. It is characterized by a rate of change that is proportional to the current value, similar to exponential growth. However, unlike exponential growth, the amount by which a quantity decreases gets smaller over time. 

##### Exponential Decay

In a graph, exponential decay is represented by a curve that becomes less steep as time progresses. This is because the quantity is decreasing, but at a slower rate as time goes on.

For example, let's consider a half-life scenario. A half-life is the time required for a quantity to reduce to half its initial value. This is a common concept in physics, especially in nuclear physics where it describes how quickly unstable atoms undergo radioactive decay. 

If a substance has a half-life of one hour, this means that every hour, half of the substance will have decayed. So, if we start with 100g of the substance, after one hour, we'll have 50g left. After two hours, we'll have 25g left, and so on. 

This can be represented by an exponential decay function. Here, the base of the exponent would be 0.5 (representing the halving each hour), and the exponent would be the number of hours.

##### Comparing Exponential Growth and Decay

When comparing exponential growth and decay, it's important to note that while exponential growth multiplies by a constant factor over each interval of time, exponential decay divides by a constant factor over each interval of time. 

This leads to a key difference between the two: while exponential growth can quickly become very large, exponential decay quickly becomes very small. 

Understanding the concepts of exponential growth and decay is crucial in algebra, as they are fundamental to many mathematical models used in science, economics, and other fields. In the next section, we will explore how to solve problems involving exponential growth and decay.

#### Subsection: Comparing exponential growth and decay

In the previous subsection, we discussed the fundamental differences between exponential growth and decay. Now, let's delve deeper into the comparison of these two concepts.

##### Exponential Growth vs Exponential Decay

Exponential growth and decay are two sides of the same coin. They both involve a quantity changing at a rate proportional to its current value. However, the direction of change is what differentiates them. 

In exponential growth, the quantity increases over time, while in exponential decay, the quantity decreases over time. This is reflected in the mathematical models of these phenomena. 

For exponential growth, the mathematical model is $y = a \cdot b^x$, where $a$ is the initial quantity, $b$ is the growth factor (greater than 1), and $x$ is the time. 

For exponential decay, the mathematical model is $y = a \cdot b^x$, where $a$ is the initial quantity, $b$ is the decay factor (between 0 and 1), and $x$ is the time.

##### Graphical Comparison

When graphed, exponential growth and decay functions have distinct shapes. 

An exponential growth function starts off slowly, then increases rapidly. The graph of an exponential growth function is a rising curve that becomes steeper as $x$ increases. 

On the other hand, an exponential decay function starts off rapidly, then decreases slowly. The graph of an exponential decay function is a falling curve that becomes less steep as $x$ increases.

##### Real-World Applications

Understanding the differences between exponential growth and decay is crucial in many fields. 

In biology, exponential growth is often used to model population growth, while exponential decay is used to model radioactive decay in physics. In finance, exponential growth models are used to calculate compound interest, while exponential decay models are used to calculate depreciation.

In conclusion, while exponential growth and decay may seem like complex concepts, they are simply different ways of describing how quantities change over time. By understanding these concepts, you can better understand and model the world around you. 

In the next section, we will explore more complex applications of exponential growth and decay, and how they can be used to solve real-world problems.

#### Subsection: Modeling with exponential decay

In this subsection, we will focus on modeling with exponential decay. As we have learned, exponential decay is a process that decreases in quantity over time at a rate proportional to its current value. This concept is used in various fields, including physics, finance, and biology.

##### Mathematical Model of Exponential Decay

The mathematical model for exponential decay is $y = a \cdot b^x$, where:

- $y$ is the quantity after time $x$,
- $a$ is the initial quantity,
- $b$ is the decay factor (between 0 and 1), and
- $x$ is the time.

The decay factor $b$ is calculated as $1 - r$, where $r$ is the rate of decay. If the rate of decay is 5%, for example, then $b = 1 - 0.05 = 0.95$.

##### Example of Exponential Decay

Let's consider an example. Suppose we have a radioactive substance with an initial quantity of 100 grams, and it decays at a rate of 2% per year. We can model this situation using the exponential decay equation.

Here, $a = 100$ (the initial quantity), $b = 1 - 0.02 = 0.98$ (the decay factor), and $x$ is the time in years. So, the equation becomes $y = 100 \cdot 0.98^x$.

After 1 year, the quantity of the substance would be $y = 100 \cdot 0.98^1 = 98$ grams. After 2 years, the quantity would be $y = 100 \cdot 0.98^2 = 96.04$ grams, and so on.

##### Graphing Exponential Decay

When graphed, an exponential decay function forms a curve that starts high and decreases over time, becoming less steep as $x$ increases. 

In our example, the graph would start at 100 (when $x = 0$) and gradually decrease. After 1 year, the point on the graph would be (1, 98), after 2 years it would be (2, 96.04), and so on.

##### Conclusion

Understanding how to model with exponential decay is a powerful tool in mathematics. It allows us to predict the future behavior of decaying quantities, whether they're radioactive substances, depreciating assets, or populations of species. 

In the next subsection, we will explore how to solve problems involving exponential decay.

#### Subsection: Writing exponential functions from tables

In this subsection, we will learn how to write exponential functions from tables. This skill is crucial in understanding and interpreting real-world situations that can be modeled by exponential functions.

##### Identifying Exponential Functions from Tables

An exponential function can be identified from a table by observing the rate of change. In an exponential function, the rate of change is not constant, but rather, it increases or decreases by a constant factor. 

For example, consider the following table:

| $x$ | $y$ |
|-----|-----|
| 0   | 2   |
| 1   | 4   |
| 2   | 8   |
| 3   | 16  |
| 4   | 32  |

In this table, the $y$-values are doubling for each increase in $x$. This is a characteristic of an exponential function.

##### Writing the Exponential Function

To write the exponential function from a table, we need to identify the initial value ($a$) and the growth or decay factor ($b$). 

The initial value is the $y$-value when $x = 0$. In our example, $a = 2$.

The growth or decay factor is the rate at which $y$ is changing for each increase in $x$. In our example, $y$ is doubling for each increase in $x$, so $b = 2$.

Therefore, the exponential function that represents the data in the table is $y = 2 \cdot 2^x$ or $y = 2^x$.

##### Example of Writing Exponential Function from Table

Let's consider another example. Suppose we have the following table:

| $x$ | $y$ |
|-----|-----|
| 0   | 5   |
| 1   | 15  |
| 2   | 45  |
| 3   | 135 |

In this table, the $y$-values are tripling for each increase in $x$. Therefore, $a = 5$ and $b = 3$. The exponential function that represents the data in the table is $y = 5 \cdot 3^x$.

##### Conclusion

Being able to write exponential functions from tables is a valuable skill in algebra. It allows us to model real-world situations and predict future behavior. In the next section, we will learn how to write exponential functions from graphs.

#### Subsection: Writing exponential functions from graphs

In this subsection, we will learn how to write exponential functions from graphs. This skill is crucial in understanding and interpreting real-world situations that can be modeled by exponential functions.

##### Identifying Exponential Functions from Graphs

An exponential function can be identified from a graph by observing the curve. In an exponential function, the curve is not a straight line, but rather, it increases or decreases at an increasing rate. 

For example, consider the following graph:

![Exponential Graph](https://www.mathsisfun.com/algebra/images/exponential-graph.svg)

In this graph, the $y$-values are doubling for each increase in $x$. This is a characteristic of an exponential function.

##### Writing the Exponential Function

To write the exponential function from a graph, we need to identify the initial value ($a$) and the growth or decay factor ($b$). 

The initial value is the $y$-value when $x = 0$. In our example, $a = 1$.

The growth or decay factor is the rate at which $y$ is changing for each increase in $x$. In our example, $y$ is doubling for each increase in $x$, so $b = 2$.

Therefore, the exponential function that represents the data in the graph is $y = 1 \cdot 2^x$ or $y = 2^x$.

##### Example of Writing Exponential Function from Graph

Let's consider another example. Suppose we have the following graph:

![Exponential Graph](https://www.mathsisfun.com/algebra/images/exponential-graph-3.svg)

In this graph, the $y$-values are tripling for each increase in $x$. Therefore, $a = 1$ and $b = 3$. The exponential function that represents the data in the graph is $y = 1 \cdot 3^x$ or $y = 3^x$.

##### Conclusion

Being able to write exponential functions from graphs is a valuable skill in algebra. It allows us to model real-world situations and predict future behavior. In the next section, we will learn how to solve exponential equations.

#### Interpreting tables and graphs of exponential functions

In the previous subsection, we learned how to write exponential functions from graphs. Now, we will learn how to interpret tables and graphs of exponential functions. This skill is equally important as it allows us to understand the behavior of the function and make predictions.

##### Interpreting Exponential Functions from Tables

A table of values for an exponential function can be identified by a constant ratio in the $y$-values for each increase in $x$. 

For example, consider the following table:

| $x$ | $y$ |
| --- | --- |
| 0   | 1   |
| 1   | 2   |
| 2   | 4   |
| 3   | 8   |

In this table, the $y$-values are doubling for each increase in $x$. This is a characteristic of an exponential function. The initial value ($a$) is 1 and the growth factor ($b$) is 2. Therefore, the exponential function that represents the data in the table is $y = 1 \cdot 2^x$ or $y = 2^x$.

##### Interpreting Exponential Functions from Graphs

As we learned in the previous subsection, an exponential function can be identified from a graph by observing the curve. The curve of an exponential function increases or decreases at an increasing rate. 

For example, consider the following graph:

![Exponential Graph](https://www.mathsisfun.com/algebra/images/exponential-graph.svg)

In this graph, the $y$-values are doubling for each increase in $x$. This is a characteristic of an exponential function. The initial value ($a$) is 1 and the growth factor ($b$) is 2. Therefore, the exponential function that represents the data in the graph is $y = 1 \cdot 2^x$ or $y = 2^x$.

##### Conclusion

Being able to interpret tables and graphs of exponential functions is a valuable skill in algebra. It allows us to understand the behavior of the function and make predictions. In the next section, we will learn how to solve exponential equations.

### Section: Exponential vs. linear models

In this section, we will compare and contrast exponential and linear models. Understanding the differences between these two types of models is crucial in algebra, as it helps us to choose the appropriate model for a given situation.

#### Linear Models

Linear models are characterized by a constant rate of change. This means that for every unit increase in the input ($x$), the output ($y$) increases or decreases by a fixed amount. 

The general form of a linear function is $y = mx + b$, where $m$ is the slope of the line (representing the rate of change), and $b$ is the $y$-intercept (representing the initial value).

For example, consider the following table:

| $x$ | $y$ |
| --- | --- |
| 0   | 1   |
| 1   | 3   |
| 2   | 5   |
| 3   | 7   |

In this table, the $y$-values are increasing by 2 for each increase in $x$. This is a characteristic of a linear function. The slope ($m$) is 2 and the $y$-intercept ($b$) is 1. Therefore, the linear function that represents the data in the table is $y = 2x + 1$.

#### Exponential Models

As we learned in the previous sections, exponential models are characterized by a constant ratio. This means that for every unit increase in the input ($x$), the output ($y$) multiplies by a fixed factor.

The general form of an exponential function is $y = ab^x$, where $a$ is the initial value and $b$ is the growth factor.

For example, consider the table we discussed in the previous section:

| $x$ | $y$ |
| --- | --- |
| 0   | 1   |
| 1   | 2   |
| 2   | 4   |
| 3   | 8   |

In this table, the $y$-values are doubling for each increase in $x$. This is a characteristic of an exponential function. The initial value ($a$) is 1 and the growth factor ($b$) is 2. Therefore, the exponential function that represents the data in the table is $y = 1 \cdot 2^x$ or $y = 2^x$.

#### Comparing Exponential and Linear Models

The key difference between linear and exponential models lies in their rates of change. Linear models have a constant rate of change, while exponential models have a constant ratio. This leads to very different behaviors for these two types of models.

In a linear model, the output increases or decreases at a constant rate, resulting in a straight line when graphed. In contrast, in an exponential model, the output increases or decreases at an increasing rate, resulting in a curve when graphed.

Understanding these differences is crucial for choosing the appropriate model for a given situation. In the next section, we will learn how to solve exponential equations.

### Section: Choosing between exponential and linear models

In the previous section, we discussed the characteristics of linear and exponential models. Now, we will delve into how to choose between these two models when faced with a real-world problem.

#### Identifying Linear Models

When you are given a problem, the first step is to identify whether it is linear or exponential. Here are some clues that suggest a problem might be linear:

1. The problem involves a constant rate of change. This means that for every unit increase in the input ($x$), the output ($y$) increases or decreases by a fixed amount.
2. The problem can be represented by a straight line when graphed.
3. The problem can be described by the equation $y = mx + b$, where $m$ and $b$ are constants.

#### Identifying Exponential Models

On the other hand, here are some clues that suggest a problem might be exponential:

1. The problem involves a constant ratio. This means that for every unit increase in the input ($x$), the output ($y$) multiplies by a fixed factor.
2. The problem can be represented by a curve when graphed.
3. The problem can be described by the equation $y = ab^x$, where $a$ and $b$ are constants.

#### Choosing the Right Model

Once you have identified whether the problem is linear or exponential, you can choose the appropriate model. Here are some considerations:

1. If the problem involves a constant rate of change, a linear model is likely the best choice.
2. If the problem involves a constant ratio, an exponential model is likely the best choice.
3. If the problem involves both a constant rate of change and a constant ratio, you may need to use a combination of linear and exponential models.

Remember, the goal is to choose the model that best represents the data or the situation. Sometimes, the choice may not be clear-cut, and you may need to try both models to see which one fits the data better.

In the next section, we will look at some examples of problems and discuss how to choose the appropriate model.

### Section: Exponential vs. linear models

In this section, we will compare and contrast exponential and linear models. Understanding the differences between these two types of models is crucial for choosing the right model for a given problem.

#### Linear Models

Linear models are characterized by a constant rate of change. This means that for every unit increase in the input ($x$), the output ($y$) increases or decreases by a fixed amount. This is represented by the equation $y = mx + b$, where $m$ is the slope of the line (representing the rate of change), and $b$ is the y-intercept (representing the initial value).

Linear models are often used to represent situations where the rate of change is constant. For example, if you are saving a fixed amount of money every month, a linear model would be a good representation of your savings over time.

#### Exponential Models

Exponential models, on the other hand, are characterized by a constant ratio. This means that for every unit increase in the input ($x$), the output ($y$) multiplies by a fixed factor. This is represented by the equation $y = ab^x$, where $a$ is the initial value and $b$ is the factor by which $y$ changes for each unit increase in $x$.

Exponential models are often used to represent situations where the rate of change is proportional to the current value. For example, if a population of bacteria is doubling every hour, an exponential model would be a good representation of the population over time.

### Subsection: Interpreting exponential and linear models

Interpreting the parameters of linear and exponential models can provide valuable insights into the problem at hand.

In a linear model, the slope $m$ represents the rate of change. A positive slope indicates that $y$ is increasing as $x$ increases, while a negative slope indicates that $y$ is decreasing as $x$ increases. The y-intercept $b$ represents the initial value of $y$ when $x$ is zero.

In an exponential model, the base $b$ represents the factor by which $y$ changes for each unit increase in $x$. If $b$ is greater than 1, $y$ is increasing as $x$ increases, while if $b$ is less than 1 but greater than 0, $y$ is decreasing as $x$ increases. The coefficient $a$ represents the initial value of $y$ when $x$ is zero.

Understanding these interpretations can help you make sense of the data and the situation, and can guide you in choosing the right model.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of exponential growth and decay in Algebra 1. We have learned that exponential growth and decay are mathematical models that describe how things grow or decrease exponentially over time. These models are not only theoretical constructs but are also applicable in various real-world scenarios such as population growth, radioactive decay, and financial investments.

We started by understanding the basic concept of exponential growth, where a quantity increases by a fixed percentage rate from one period to the next. We represented this mathematically using the formula $y = a(1 + r)^t$, where $y$ is the final amount, $a$ is the initial amount, $r$ is the rate of growth, and $t$ is the time.

Next, we delved into exponential decay, which is the opposite of exponential growth. Here, a quantity decreases by a fixed percentage rate from one period to the next. We represented this using the formula $y = a(1 - r)^t$.

We also learned how to graph exponential growth and decay functions, and how to interpret these graphs. We saw that the graph of an exponential growth function is an upward-sloping curve, while the graph of an exponential decay function is a downward-sloping curve.

Finally, we discussed the concept of the half-life in the context of exponential decay, and the doubling time in the context of exponential growth. These are important concepts that help us understand how quickly a quantity is growing or decaying.

In conclusion, understanding exponential growth and decay is crucial in Algebra 1 as it forms the basis for more complex mathematical models. It is also a powerful tool that can help us make sense of various phenomena in the world around us.

We started by understanding the basic concept of exponential growth, where a quantity increases by a fixed percentage rate from one period to the next. We represented this mathematically using the formula $y = a(1 + r)^t$, where $y$ is the final amount, $a$ is the initial amount, $r$ is the rate of growth, and $t$ is the time.

Next, we delved into exponential decay, which is the opposite of exponential growth. Here, a quantity decreases by a fixed percentage rate from one period to the next. We represented this using the formula $y = a(1 - r)^t$.

We also learned how to graph exponential growth and decay functions, and how to interpret these graphs. We saw that the graph of an exponential growth function is an upward-sloping curve, while the graph of an exponential decay function is a downward-sloping curve.

Finally, we discussed the concept of the half-life in the context of exponential decay, and the doubling time in the context of exponential growth. These are important concepts that help us understand how quickly a quantity is growing or decaying.
