## Chapter: Quadratic functions & equations

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Quadratic Functions & Equations. This chapter is designed to help you master the fundamental concepts and techniques associated with quadratic functions and equations, which are a cornerstone of Algebra 1. 

Quadratic functions and equations are a significant part of algebra and are used to model various real-world situations. They are characterized by their highest exponent of 2, which gives them a unique U-shaped graph known as a parabola. In this chapter, we will delve into the intricacies of quadratic functions and equations, exploring their structure, properties, and applications.

We will start by defining quadratic functions and equations, and understanding their general form `$ax^2 + bx + c = 0$`, where `$a$`, `$b$`, and `$c$` are constants. We will then explore the concept of the vertex and axis of symmetry of a parabola, which are key to graphing quadratic functions.

Next, we will learn about the different methods to solve quadratic equations, including factoring, completing the square, and the quadratic formula `$x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$`. Each method has its own advantages and is suitable for different types of quadratic equations.

Finally, we will apply these concepts to solve real-world problems. Quadratic functions and equations are used in various fields such as physics, engineering, and economics to model scenarios like projectile motion, optimization problems, and more.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of quadratic functions and equations, and be able to solve and apply them with confidence. So, let's dive in and start mastering quadratic functions and equations!

## Chapter: Quadratic functions & equations

### Section: Intro to parabolas

Parabolas are the graphical representation of quadratic functions. They are U-shaped curves that can open upwards or downwards depending on the value of the leading coefficient `$a$` in the quadratic function `$f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$`. 

#### Understanding parabolas

A parabola is defined as the set of all points that are equidistant from a fixed point, called the focus, and a fixed line, called the directrix. However, in the context of quadratic functions, we often refer to parabolas as the graphs of these functions.

The vertex of a parabola is the point where the parabola turns; it is the highest or lowest point on the graph, depending on whether the parabola opens upwards or downwards. The vertex can be found using the formula `$(h, k) = \left(-\frac{b}{2a}, f\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right)\right)$`, where `$h$` and `$k$` are the x and y coordinates of the vertex, respectively.

The axis of symmetry of a parabola is the vertical line that passes through the vertex of the parabola. It divides the parabola into two mirror images. The equation of the axis of symmetry is `$x = h$`, where `$h$` is the x-coordinate of the vertex.

The direction in which a parabola opens depends on the sign of `$a$`. If `$a > 0$`, the parabola opens upwards, and the vertex is the minimum point. If `$a < 0$`, the parabola opens downwards, and the vertex is the maximum point.

The graph of a quadratic function is always a parabola. By understanding the properties of parabolas, we can graph any quadratic function and solve a wide range of problems in various fields. In the next sections, we will delve deeper into the properties of parabolas and learn how to graph quadratic functions.

### Section: Graphing parabolas

Graphing a parabola involves understanding its key features and using them to plot the curve accurately. These features include the vertex, the axis of symmetry, and the direction of opening, which we have already discussed in the previous section. Now, let's learn how to use these features to graph a parabola.

#### Step 1: Identify the key features

Given a quadratic function in the form `$f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$`, the first step is to identify the key features of the parabola. 

- The vertex `$(h, k)$` can be found using the formula `$(h, k) = \left(-\frac{b}{2a}, f\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right)\right)$`.
- The axis of symmetry is the vertical line `$x = h$`.
- The direction of opening is determined by the sign of `$a$`. If `$a > 0$`, the parabola opens upwards. If `$a < 0$`, the parabola opens downwards.

#### Step 2: Plot the vertex and the axis of symmetry

Once you have identified the vertex and the axis of symmetry, plot them on a graph. The vertex is a point on the graph, and the axis of symmetry is a vertical line passing through the vertex.

#### Step 3: Determine the direction of opening

The direction of opening of the parabola is determined by the sign of `$a$`. If `$a > 0$`, draw the parabola opening upwards. If `$a < 0$`, draw the parabola opening downwards.

#### Step 4: Plot other points

To make the graph more accurate, you can plot additional points. One way to do this is to choose a few x-values, calculate the corresponding y-values using the function `$f(x)$`, and plot these points on the graph. Make sure to choose x-values on both sides of the axis of symmetry to ensure that the graph is symmetrical.

#### Step 5: Draw the parabola

Finally, draw the parabola by connecting the points. Make sure that the shape of the parabola is a smooth curve and that it opens in the correct direction.

By following these steps, you can graph any quadratic function. Remember, the graph of a quadratic function is always a parabola, and understanding the properties of parabolas is key to graphing these functions accurately. In the next sections, we will explore more properties of parabolas and learn how to use them to solve quadratic equations.

### Section: Intro to parabolas

A parabola is a curve that is formed by the graph of a quadratic function. It is a U-shaped curve that can open upwards or downwards depending on the sign of the coefficient of the quadratic term. In this section, we will delve deeper into the properties of parabolas and how to interpret their graphs.

#### Subsection: Interpreting graphs of parabolas

Interpreting the graph of a parabola involves understanding its key features and how they relate to the quadratic function it represents. Here are some key features to look for:

- **Vertex**: The vertex of a parabola is the point where it reaches its maximum or minimum value. In the graph of a quadratic function, the vertex corresponds to the maximum or minimum value of the function. If the parabola opens upwards, the vertex is the minimum point. If the parabola opens downwards, the vertex is the maximum point.

- **Axis of Symmetry**: The axis of symmetry is a vertical line that passes through the vertex of the parabola. It divides the parabola into two mirror images. The equation of the axis of symmetry is `$x = h$`, where `$h$` is the x-coordinate of the vertex.

- **Direction of Opening**: The direction in which a parabola opens depends on the sign of the coefficient of the quadratic term (`$a$`) in the quadratic function. If `$a > 0$`, the parabola opens upwards. If `$a < 0$`, the parabola opens downwards.

- **Y-intercept**: The y-intercept is the point where the parabola crosses the y-axis. This point corresponds to the value of the function when `$x = 0$`.

- **X-intercepts (or roots)**: The x-intercepts are the points where the parabola crosses the x-axis. These points correspond to the solutions of the quadratic equation `$ax^2 + bx + c = 0$`.

By understanding these key features, you can interpret the graph of a parabola and understand the behavior of the quadratic function it represents. In the next section, we will learn how to solve quadratic equations, which will further enhance our understanding of parabolas.

### Section: Solving and graphing with factored form

In the previous section, we learned about the key features of a parabola and how to interpret its graph. Now, we will delve into the factored form of a quadratic function and how it can be used to solve quadratic equations and graph parabolas.

#### Subsection: Understanding factored form

The factored form of a quadratic function is given by the equation `$f(x) = a(x - r)(x - s)$`, where `$a$` is the coefficient of the quadratic term, and `$r$` and `$s$` are the roots of the equation. The roots of the equation are the x-values for which the function equals zero. In other words, they are the x-intercepts of the parabola.

The factored form is particularly useful for solving quadratic equations because it allows us to easily find the roots of the equation. If `$f(x) = a(x - r)(x - s) = 0$`, then either `$x - r = 0$` or `$x - s = 0$`, which means that `$x = r$` or `$x = s$`.

#### Subsection: Graphing with factored form

The factored form also provides valuable information for graphing the parabola. Here's how:

- **X-intercepts (or roots)**: As mentioned above, the roots of the equation `$r$` and `$s$` are the x-intercepts of the parabola. These are the points where the parabola crosses the x-axis.

- **Axis of Symmetry**: The axis of symmetry is the vertical line that passes through the midpoint of the line segment connecting the x-intercepts. In other words, it is the line `$x = \frac{r + s}{2}$`.

- **Vertex**: The vertex of the parabola is the point where it reaches its maximum or minimum value. Since the parabola is symmetric about the axis of symmetry, the x-coordinate of the vertex is also `$\frac{r + s}{2}$`. To find the y-coordinate of the vertex, we substitute this value into the function: `$f(\frac{r + s}{2})$`.

- **Direction of Opening**: The direction in which the parabola opens depends on the sign of the coefficient `$a$`. If `$a > 0$`, the parabola opens upwards. If `$a < 0$`, the parabola opens downwards.

By understanding the factored form of a quadratic function, you can easily solve quadratic equations and graph parabolas. In the next section, we will learn about the vertex form of a quadratic function, which provides even more information about the graph of a parabola.

#### Subsection: Solving quadratic equations in factored form

Now that we understand the factored form and how it can be used to graph a parabola, let's use it to solve quadratic equations. 

Given a quadratic equation in factored form, `$f(x) = a(x - r)(x - s)$`, we can find the roots of the equation by setting `$f(x)$` equal to zero and solving for `$x$`. This gives us the equation `$a(x - r)(x - s) = 0$`.

To solve this equation, we use the zero product property, which states that if the product of two numbers is zero, then at least one of the numbers must be zero. Therefore, either `$x - r = 0$` or `$x - s = 0$`. Solving these equations gives us `$x = r$` or `$x = s$`, which are the roots of the quadratic equation.

Let's look at an example:

Consider the quadratic equation `$f(x) = 2(x - 3)(x + 4) = 0$`. To find the roots, we set each factor equal to zero:

1. `$x - 3 = 0$` gives `$x = 3$`
2. `$x + 4 = 0$` gives `$x = -4$`

So, the roots of the equation are `$x = 3$` and `$x = -4$`.

#### Subsection: Checking solutions

After finding the roots, it's always a good idea to check your solutions by substituting them back into the original equation. 

For our example, if we substitute `$x = 3$` into the equation, we get:

`$f(3) = 2(3 - 3)(3 + 4) = 2(0)(7) = 0$`

And if we substitute `$x = -4$`, we get:

`$f(-4) = 2(-4 - 3)(-4 + 4) = 2(-7)(0) = 0$`

In both cases, the left-hand side equals the right-hand side, confirming that `$x = 3$` and `$x = -4$` are indeed the roots of the equation.

In the next section, we will learn about the vertex form of a quadratic function, which provides another way to graph and solve quadratic equations.

### Section: Solving and graphing with factored form

#### Subsection: Graphing quadratic functions in factored form

Now that we have learned how to solve quadratic equations in factored form, let's use this knowledge to graph these functions. 

Given a quadratic function in factored form, `$f(x) = a(x - r)(x - s)$`, the roots `$r$` and `$s$` are the x-intercepts of the graph. The x-intercepts are the points where the graph crosses the x-axis, which are the solutions to the equation `$f(x) = 0$`.

The vertex of the parabola is the point `$(h, k)$` where `$h = \frac{r + s}{2}$` and `$k = f(h)$`. This is the highest or lowest point of the parabola, depending on whether the parabola opens upwards (`$a > 0$`) or downwards (`$a < 0$`).

Let's graph the quadratic function from our previous example: `$f(x) = 2(x - 3)(x + 4)$`.

1. The roots are `$x = 3$` and `$x = -4$`, so the graph crosses the x-axis at these points.
2. The vertex is at `$(h, k)$` where `$h = \frac{3 + (-4)}{2} = -0.5$` and `$k = f(-0.5) = 2(-0.5 - 3)(-0.5 + 4) = 2(-3.5)(3.5) = -24.5$`. So, the vertex is at `(-0.5, -24.5)`.
3. Since `$a = 2 > 0$`, the parabola opens upwards.

By plotting the x-intercepts and the vertex, and knowing the direction of the parabola, we can sketch the graph of the quadratic function.

In the next section, we will learn about the vertex form of a quadratic function, which provides another way to graph and solve quadratic equations.

### Section: Solving by taking the square root

In the previous sections, we have learned how to solve and graph quadratic equations in factored form. Now, we will explore another method of solving quadratic equations: by taking the square root.

#### Subsection: Understanding the square root method

The square root method is used to solve quadratic equations that can be rearranged into the form `$x^2 = c$`, where `$c$` is a constant. The solutions to this equation are `$x = \sqrt{c}$` and `$x = -\sqrt{c}$`. This is because both of these values, when squared, will give the value of `$c$`.

Let's consider an example: `$x^2 = 16$`. 

To solve this equation, we take the square root of both sides. The square root of `$x^2$` is `$x$`, and the square root of `$16$` is `$4$`. So, `$x = \sqrt{16}$` gives us `$x = 4$`. 

However, we must remember that `$-4$` is also a solution, because `$$(-4)^2 = 16$$`. So, the solutions to the equation `$x^2 = 16$` are `$x = 4$` and `$x = -4$`.

This method is straightforward and efficient when the quadratic equation can be written in the form `$x^2 = c$`. However, not all quadratic equations can be easily rearranged into this form. In the next section, we will learn about the quadratic formula, a method that can be used to solve any quadratic equation.

#### Subsection: Steps to solve quadratic equations by taking the square root

To solve a quadratic equation by taking the square root, follow these steps:

1. **Rewrite the equation in the form `$x^2 = c$`**: This is the standard form for solving quadratic equations by taking the square root. If the equation is not already in this form, you may need to use algebraic operations such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division to rearrange it.

2. **Take the square root of both sides**: Once the equation is in the form `$x^2 = c$`, take the square root of both sides. Remember, the square root of `$x^2$` is `$x$`, and the square root of `$c$` will be a constant value.

3. **Find the positive and negative solutions**: The solutions to the equation are `$x = \sqrt{c}$` and `$x = -\sqrt{c}$`. This is because both of these values, when squared, will give the value of `$c$`.

Let's consider an example: `$x^2 = 25$`.

1. The equation is already in the form `$x^2 = c$`.

2. Taking the square root of both sides gives us `$x = \sqrt{25}$`.

3. The solutions to the equation are `$x = 5$` and `$x = -5$`, because both `$(5)^2 = 25$` and `$$(-5)^2 = 25$$`.

Remember, this method is most effective when the quadratic equation can be easily rearranged into the form `$x^2 = c$`. If the equation cannot be rearranged into this form, other methods such as factoring or the quadratic formula may be more appropriate.

In the next section, we will explore the quadratic formula, a powerful tool that can be used to solve any quadratic equation.

#### Subsection: Checking solutions to quadratic equations

After solving a quadratic equation, it is important to check your solutions to ensure they are correct. This is done by substituting the solutions back into the original equation and verifying that both sides of the equation are equal. 

Let's consider the example from the previous section: `$x^2 = 25$`. We found the solutions to be `$x = 5$` and `$x = -5$`.

To check these solutions, we substitute them back into the original equation:

1. **Substitute `$x = 5$` into the equation**: This gives us `$(5)^2 = 25$`, which simplifies to `$25 = 25$`. Since both sides of the equation are equal, `$x = 5$` is a valid solution.

2. **Substitute `$x = -5$` into the equation**: This gives us `$$(-5)^2 = 25$$`, which simplifies to `$25 = 25$`. Since both sides of the equation are equal, `$x = -5$` is also a valid solution.

If, after substituting a solution back into the equation, both sides of the equation are not equal, then that solution is not valid. This could indicate a mistake in your calculations or an error in the original equation.

Checking your solutions is a crucial step in solving quadratic equations. It not only confirms the accuracy of your solutions but also helps you understand the process of solving quadratic equations more deeply. Always remember to check your solutions to ensure they are correct. 

In the next section, we will delve into the quadratic formula, a powerful method that can solve any quadratic equation.

### Section: Vertex form

The vertex form of a quadratic function is a special form that provides a lot of information about the function, including its vertex, axis of symmetry, and direction of opening. The vertex form of a quadratic function is given by:

$$
f(x) = a(x-h)^2 + k
$$

where `(h, k)` is the vertex of the parabola, `a` is a non-zero constant that determines the direction and the width of the parabola. If `a` is positive, the parabola opens upwards, and if `a` is negative, the parabola opens downwards. 

#### Subsection: Understanding vertex form

To understand the vertex form of a quadratic function, let's consider an example. 

Suppose we have a quadratic function given by:

$$
f(x) = 2(x-3)^2 + 4
$$

In this function, the vertex `(h, k)` is `(3, 4)`. This means that the parabola represented by this function has its vertex at the point `(3, 4)`. 

The value of `a` is `2`, which is positive. This tells us that the parabola opens upwards. 

The vertex form of a quadratic function is particularly useful because it allows us to easily identify key features of the function. By simply looking at the function in vertex form, we can determine the vertex, the direction of opening, and the axis of symmetry of the parabola. 

In the next section, we will learn how to convert a quadratic function from standard form to vertex form. This is a useful skill that will allow us to more easily analyze and graph quadratic functions.

### Section: Writing quadratic functions in vertex form

In the previous section, we learned about the vertex form of a quadratic function and how it can provide us with valuable information about the function. Now, we will learn how to write a quadratic function in vertex form. 

#### Subsection: Converting from standard form to vertex form

The standard form of a quadratic function is given by:

$$
f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c
$$

where `a`, `b`, and `c` are constants. To convert a quadratic function from standard form to vertex form, we use a process called "completing the square". 

Let's consider an example. Suppose we have a quadratic function in standard form:

$$
f(x) = 2x^2 - 12x + 20
$$

To convert this function to vertex form, we first factor out the coefficient of the `x^2` term from the first two terms:

$$
f(x) = 2(x^2 - 6x) + 20
$$

Next, we complete the square inside the parentheses. To do this, we take half of the coefficient of `x`, square it, and add and subtract it inside the parentheses:

$$
f(x) = 2[(x^2 - 6x + 9) - 9] + 20
$$

This simplifies to:

$$
f(x) = 2[(x - 3)^2 - 9] + 20
$$

Finally, we simplify the expression to obtain the function in vertex form:

$$
f(x) = 2(x - 3)^2 + 2
$$

So, the vertex form of the function `f(x) = 2x^2 - 12x + 20` is `f(x) = 2(x - 3)^2 + 2`.

In the next section, we will learn how to graph quadratic functions in vertex form. This will allow us to visually represent the function and better understand its properties.

### Section: Vertex form

The vertex form of a quadratic function is given by:

$$
f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k
$$

where `a`, `h`, and `k` are constants. The vertex of the function is the point `(h, k)`, and `a` determines the direction and the width of the parabola. If `a` is positive, the parabola opens upwards, and if `a` is negative, the parabola opens downwards. The absolute value of `a` determines the width of the parabola: the larger the absolute value of `a`, the narrower the parabola, and the smaller the absolute value of `a`, the wider the parabola.

### Subsection: Graphing quadratic functions in vertex form

To graph a quadratic function in vertex form, we follow these steps:

1. Identify the vertex `(h, k)`. This is the point where the parabola turns. Plot this point on the graph.

2. Determine the direction of the parabola by looking at the sign of `a`. If `a` is positive, the parabola opens upwards. If `a` is negative, the parabola opens downwards.

3. Find the axis of symmetry, which is the vertical line that passes through the vertex. The equation of the axis of symmetry is `x = h`.

4. Choose a few points on either side of the axis of symmetry and calculate their corresponding `y` values using the function. Plot these points on the graph.

5. Draw a smooth curve through the points to complete the graph of the function.

Let's consider an example. Suppose we have a quadratic function in vertex form:

$$
f(x) = 2(x - 3)^2 + 2
$$

The vertex of the function is `(3, 2)`. Since `a = 2` is positive, the parabola opens upwards. The axis of symmetry is `x = 3`.

We can choose a few points on either side of `x = 3` and calculate their corresponding `y` values:

- For `x = 2`, `f(x) = 2(2 - 3)^2 + 2 = 4`
- For `x = 4`, `f(x) = 2(4 - 3)^2 + 2 = 4`
- For `x = 1`, `f(x) = 2(1 - 3)^2 + 2 = 10`
- For `x = 5`, `f(x) = 2(5 - 3)^2 + 2 = 10`

We plot these points and the vertex on the graph, and draw a smooth curve through the points to complete the graph of the function.

Graphing quadratic functions in vertex form allows us to visually represent the function and better understand its properties. In the next section, we will learn about the properties of quadratic functions and how they relate to the graph of the function.

### Section: Solving quadratics by factoring

Factoring is a powerful method for solving quadratic equations. A quadratic equation is an equation of the form:

$$
ax^2 + bx + c = 0
$$

where `a`, `b`, and `c` are constants and `a ≠ 0`. The solutions to the equation are the values of `x` that make the equation true.

#### Subsection: Solving quadratic equations by factoring

To solve a quadratic equation by factoring, we first need to write the equation in the form `(px + q)(rx + s) = 0`, where `p`, `q`, `r`, and `s` are constants. This is done by factoring the quadratic expression on the left side of the equation.

Once the equation is in this form, we can use the Zero Product Property, which states that if the product of two numbers is zero, then at least one of the numbers must be zero. This means that either `px + q = 0` or `rx + s = 0`.

Solving these two equations gives us the solutions to the original quadratic equation.

Let's consider an example. Suppose we have the quadratic equation:

$$
x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0
$$

We can factor the left side of the equation as `(x - 2)(x - 3) = 0`. 

According to the Zero Product Property, either `x - 2 = 0` or `x - 3 = 0`. Solving these equations gives `x = 2` and `x = 3` as the solutions to the original quadratic equation.

In the next section, we will explore another method for solving quadratic equations: the Quadratic Formula.

### Section: Checking solutions to quadratic equations

After solving a quadratic equation, it's important to check your solutions to ensure they are correct. This is done by substituting the solutions back into the original equation and verifying that they satisfy the equation.

#### Subsection: Checking solutions by substitution

Let's continue with the example from the previous section. We found the solutions to the equation $x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$ to be $x = 2$ and $x = 3$. To check these solutions, we substitute them back into the original equation.

For $x = 2$, the equation becomes:

$$
(2)^2 - 5(2) + 6 = 0
$$

Simplifying this gives $4 - 10 + 6 = 0$, which is indeed true. So, $x = 2$ is a valid solution.

For $x = 3$, the equation becomes:

$$
(3)^2 - 5(3) + 6 = 0
$$

Simplifying this gives $9 - 15 + 6 = 0$, which is also true. So, $x = 3$ is a valid solution as well.

This process of checking solutions is crucial in ensuring the accuracy of your work. It also helps to understand the nature of quadratic equations and how their solutions work.

In the next section, we will explore the Quadratic Formula, another powerful method for solving quadratic equations.

### Section: Solving quadratics by factoring

Factoring is another method used to solve quadratic equations. It involves expressing the quadratic equation in a form that can be factored into two binomial expressions. The solutions to the equation are then found by setting each factor equal to zero and solving for $x$.

Let's consider the quadratic equation $x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$. This equation can be factored into $(x - 2)(x - 3) = 0$. 

Setting each factor equal to zero gives us:

$$
x - 2 = 0 \quad \text{and} \quad x - 3 = 0
$$

Solving these equations gives us the solutions $x = 2$ and $x = 3$, which we already verified in the previous section.

Factoring is a powerful method for solving quadratic equations, but it requires a good understanding of algebraic expressions and their properties. It's also worth noting that not all quadratic equations can be easily factored, especially if the coefficients are not integers or if the equation has no real solutions.

### Subsection: Real-world applications of solving by factoring

Quadratic equations are not just abstract mathematical concepts; they have many real-world applications. For example, they can be used to model the trajectory of a thrown object, the growth of a population, or the profit of a business.

Let's consider a business scenario. Suppose a company's profit, $P$, in thousands of dollars, can be modeled by the equation $P = -2x^2 + 20x - 30$, where $x$ is the number of units produced and sold. The company wants to find out how many units it needs to sell to maximize its profit.

This is a quadratic equation, and we can solve it by factoring. First, we rewrite the equation in standard form:

$$
-2x^2 + 20x - 30 = 0
$$

This equation can be factored into $-2(x - 5)(x - 3) = 0$. Setting each factor equal to zero gives us:

$$
x - 5 = 0 \quad \text{and} \quad x - 3 = 0
$$

Solving these equations gives us $x = 5$ and $x = 3$. However, since the coefficient of $x^2$ is negative, the graph of the equation is a downward-opening parabola, and the maximum value occurs at the vertex. The $x$-coordinate of the vertex of a parabola given by the equation $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ is $-\frac{b}{2a}$, so in this case, the number of units that maximizes profit is $-\frac{20}{-4} = 5$.

So, the company should aim to produce and sell 5,000 units to maximize its profit. This is just one example of how solving quadratic equations by factoring can be applied in the real world.

### Section: The quadratic formula

While factoring is a powerful method for solving quadratic equations, it is not always possible or practical, especially when the coefficients are not integers or if the equation has no real solutions. In such cases, we can use the quadratic formula, which provides a general solution for all quadratic equations.

The quadratic formula is derived from the process of completing the square, and it is given by:

$$
x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}
$$

where $a$, $b$, and $c$ are the coefficients of the quadratic equation in the form $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$.

The term under the square root, $b^2 - 4ac$, is known as the discriminant. It determines the nature of the roots of the quadratic equation:

- If the discriminant is positive, the equation has two distinct real roots.
- If the discriminant is zero, the equation has exactly one real root (or a repeated root).
- If the discriminant is negative, the equation has two complex roots.

Let's consider an example. Suppose we have the quadratic equation $2x^2 - 3x - 2 = 0$. The coefficients are $a = 2$, $b = -3$, and $c = -2$. 

Substituting these values into the quadratic formula gives us:

$$
x = \frac{-(-3) \pm \sqrt{(-3)^2 - 4*2*(-2)}}{2*2}
$$

Solving this equation gives us the solutions $x = 1$ and $x = -1$, which we can verify by substituting these values back into the original equation.

### Subsection: Understanding the quadratic formula

The quadratic formula might seem complex at first, but it's actually quite straightforward once you understand what each part of the formula does.

- The $-b$ term shifts the equation along the x-axis.
- The $\pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}$ term determines how far the roots are spread apart.
- The $2a$ term scales the equation along the x-axis.

The quadratic formula is a powerful tool for solving quadratic equations, and it's a key part of understanding quadratic functions and equations. It's also worth noting that the quadratic formula works for all quadratic equations, not just those that can be factored easily. This makes it a very versatile method for solving these types of equations.

### Section: Solving quadratic equations with the quadratic formula

The quadratic formula is a powerful tool that can solve any quadratic equation, regardless of the coefficients. Let's dive deeper into how to use it to solve quadratic equations.

#### Subsection: Steps to solve quadratic equations using the quadratic formula

1. **Identify the coefficients:** The first step in using the quadratic formula is to identify the coefficients $a$, $b$, and $c$ in the quadratic equation. The quadratic equation is generally written in the form $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$.

2. **Substitute the coefficients into the quadratic formula:** Once you have identified the coefficients, substitute them into the quadratic formula: $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$.

3. **Calculate the discriminant:** The discriminant is the part of the formula under the square root, $b^2 - 4ac$. Calculate the value of the discriminant to determine the nature of the roots.

4. **Solve for $x$:** Finally, solve the equation for $x$. Depending on the value of the discriminant, you may have one, two, or no real solutions.

Let's illustrate these steps with an example.

#### Subsection: Example of solving a quadratic equation using the quadratic formula

Consider the quadratic equation $3x^2 - 4x - 7 = 0$. Here, $a = 3$, $b = -4$, and $c = -7$.

Substitute these values into the quadratic formula:

$$
x = \frac{-(-4) \pm \sqrt{(-4)^2 - 4*3*(-7)}}{2*3}
$$

This simplifies to:

$$
x = \frac{4 \pm \sqrt{16 + 84}}{6}
$$

Further simplifying gives:

$$
x = \frac{4 \pm \sqrt{100}}{6} = \frac{4 \pm 10}{6}
$$

So, the solutions are $x = 7/3$ and $x = -1$.

You can verify these solutions by substituting them back into the original equation. For example, substituting $x = 7/3$ into the equation gives:

$$
3*(7/3)^2 - 4*(7/3) - 7 = 0
$$

which simplifies to $0 = 0$, confirming that $x = 7/3$ is indeed a solution.

The quadratic formula is a powerful tool for solving quadratic equations, and understanding how to use it is a key part of mastering algebra. With practice, you'll be able to solve any quadratic equation quickly and accurately.

### Section: The quadratic formula

#### Subsection: Using the discriminant to analyze solutions

In the previous section, we introduced the concept of the discriminant, which is the part of the quadratic formula under the square root, $b^2 - 4ac$. The discriminant plays a crucial role in determining the nature of the solutions of a quadratic equation. Let's delve deeper into how to use the discriminant to analyze solutions.

The discriminant can take on three types of values: positive, zero, or negative. Each of these values tells us something different about the solutions of the quadratic equation.

1. **Positive discriminant:** If the discriminant is positive, the quadratic equation has two distinct real solutions. This is because the square root of a positive number is a real number, and the $\pm$ in the quadratic formula indicates two different solutions.

2. **Zero discriminant:** If the discriminant is zero, the quadratic equation has exactly one real solution. This is because the square root of zero is zero, so the $\pm$ does not create two different solutions.

3. **Negative discriminant:** If the discriminant is negative, the quadratic equation has no real solutions. This is because the square root of a negative number is not a real number.

Let's illustrate these concepts with examples.

#### Subsection: Examples of using the discriminant to analyze solutions

**Example 1:** Consider the quadratic equation $x^2 - 4x + 4 = 0$. Here, $a = 1$, $b = -4$, and $c = 4$.

The discriminant is $(-4)^2 - 4*1*4 = 16 - 16 = 0$. Since the discriminant is zero, this equation has exactly one real solution.

**Example 2:** Consider the quadratic equation $x^2 + 2x + 1 = 0$. Here, $a = 1$, $b = 2$, and $c = 1$.

The discriminant is $(2)^2 - 4*1*1 = 4 - 4 = 0$. Again, since the discriminant is zero, this equation has exactly one real solution.

**Example 3:** Consider the quadratic equation $x^2 + x + 1 = 0$. Here, $a = 1$, $b = 1$, and $c = 1$.

The discriminant is $(1)^2 - 4*1*1 = 1 - 4 = -3$. Since the discriminant is negative, this equation has no real solutions.

Understanding the discriminant and how it affects the solutions of a quadratic equation is a key part of mastering algebra. It allows us to quickly determine the nature of the solutions without having to fully solve the equation.

### Section: Completing the square intro

Completing the square is a method used to solve quadratic equations, rewrite quadratic functions, and evaluate integrals. This technique is fundamental in algebra and calculus. It is called "completing the square" because it involves adding a constant to a quadratic expression to turn it into a perfect square trinomial.

#### Subsection: Understanding completing the square

To understand the process of completing the square, let's start with a quadratic equation in the form of $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$. The goal is to rewrite this equation in the form of $(x - h)^2 = k$, where $h$ and $k$ are constants. This form is easier to solve and provides useful information about the graph of the function.

Here are the steps to complete the square:

1. **Make sure the coefficient of $x^2$ is 1:** If the coefficient of $x^2$ is not 1, divide the entire equation by the coefficient of $x^2$.

2. **Move the constant term to the right side of the equation:** Subtract $c$ from both sides of the equation to isolate the quadratic terms on the left side.

3. **Complete the square on the left side of the equation and balance this by adding the same value to the right side of the equation:** To do this, take half of the coefficient of $x$, square it, and add it to both sides of the equation. This creates a perfect square trinomial on the left side of the equation.

4. **Factor the left side of the equation:** The left side of the equation is now a perfect square trinomial, which can be factored into $(x - h)^2$.

5. **Solve for $x$:** Take the square root of both sides of the equation and solve for $x$.

In the next section, we will illustrate these steps with examples.

### Section: Completing the square to solve quadratic equations

In this section, we will apply the steps of completing the square to solve quadratic equations. We will walk through a few examples to illustrate the process.

#### Example 1: Solve $x^2 + 6x + 8 = 0$

1. **Make sure the coefficient of $x^2$ is 1:** In this case, the coefficient of $x^2$ is already 1, so we can move to the next step.

2. **Move the constant term to the right side of the equation:** Subtract 8 from both sides to get $x^2 + 6x = -8$.

3. **Complete the square on the left side of the equation and balance this by adding the same value to the right side of the equation:** Half of the coefficient of $x$ is 3, and squaring this gives 9. Add 9 to both sides to get $x^2 + 6x + 9 = 1$.

4. **Factor the left side of the equation:** The left side factors to $(x + 3)^2$.

5. **Solve for $x$:** Take the square root of both sides to get $x + 3 = \pm 1$. Solving for $x$ gives $x = -3 \pm 1$, so the solutions are $x = -4$ and $x = -2$.

#### Example 2: Solve $2x^2 - 4x - 6 = 0$

1. **Make sure the coefficient of $x^2$ is 1:** Divide the entire equation by 2 to get $x^2 - 2x - 3 = 0$.

2. **Move the constant term to the right side of the equation:** Add 3 to both sides to get $x^2 - 2x = 3$.

3. **Complete the square on the left side of the equation and balance this by adding the same value to the right side of the equation:** Half of the coefficient of $x$ is -1, and squaring this gives 1. Add 1 to both sides to get $x^2 - 2x + 1 = 4$.

4. **Factor the left side of the equation:** The left side factors to $(x - 1)^2$.

5. **Solve for $x$:** Take the square root of both sides to get $x - 1 = \pm 2$. Solving for $x$ gives $x = 1 \pm 2$, so the solutions are $x = -1$ and $x = 3$.

These examples illustrate how completing the square can be used to solve quadratic equations. Practice this method with different equations to become more comfortable with the process. In the next section, we will explore how completing the square can be used to rewrite quadratic functions.

### Section: Completing the square intro

Completing the square is a method used to solve quadratic equations, but it also serves another important purpose: it allows us to rewrite quadratic functions in a form that makes it easy to identify the vertex of the parabola. This form is known as the vertex form of a quadratic function.

A quadratic function is generally written in the form $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$, where $a$, $b$, and $c$ are constants. The vertex form of a quadratic function is $f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$, where $(h, k)$ is the vertex of the parabola.

### Subsection: Using completing the square to write quadratic functions in vertex form

To rewrite a quadratic function in vertex form, we use the method of completing the square. Let's illustrate this with an example.

#### Example: Write $f(x) = x^2 - 6x + 8$ in vertex form

1. **Make sure the coefficient of $x^2$ is 1:** In this case, the coefficient of $x^2$ is already 1, so we can move to the next step.

2. **Move the constant term to the right side of the equation:** Subtract 8 from both sides to get $x^2 - 6x = -8$.

3. **Complete the square on the left side of the equation and balance this by adding the same value to the right side of the equation:** Half of the coefficient of $x$ is -3, and squaring this gives 9. Add 9 to both sides to get $x^2 - 6x + 9 = 1$.

4. **Factor the left side of the equation:** The left side factors to $(x - 3)^2$.

5. **Write the equation in vertex form:** We now have $f(x) = (x - 3)^2 + 1$.

So, the vertex form of the function $f(x) = x^2 - 6x + 8$ is $f(x) = (x - 3)^2 + 1$. This tells us that the vertex of the parabola is at $(3, 1)$.

By practicing this method, you can become proficient at rewriting quadratic functions in vertex form. This will be a valuable skill when you study the graphing of quadratic functions, as it will allow you to easily identify the vertex of the parabola.

### Section: More on completing the square

In the previous section, we learned how to complete the square to rewrite a quadratic function in vertex form. This method is not only useful for identifying the vertex of a parabola, but it also provides a way to solve quadratic equations that cannot be factored easily. In this section, we will delve deeper into the process of completing the square, and we will also explore how to handle cases where the coefficient of $x^2$ is not 1.

#### Subsection: Completing the square when the coefficient of $x^2$ is not 1

When the coefficient of $x^2$ is not 1, we need to adjust our approach slightly. Let's illustrate this with an example.

#### Example: Write $f(x) = 2x^2 - 8x + 6$ in vertex form

1. **Divide the entire equation by the coefficient of $x^2$:** In this case, we divide the entire equation by 2 to get $x^2 - 4x + 3 = 0$.

2. **Move the constant term to the right side of the equation:** Subtract 3 from both sides to get $x^2 - 4x = -3$.

3. **Complete the square on the left side of the equation and balance this by adding the same value to the right side of the equation:** Half of the coefficient of $x$ is -2, and squaring this gives 4. Add 4 to both sides to get $x^2 - 4x + 4 = 1$.

4. **Factor the left side of the equation:** The left side factors to $(x - 2)^2$.

5. **Write the equation in vertex form:** We now have $f(x) = 2(x - 2)^2 + 1$.

So, the vertex form of the function $f(x) = 2x^2 - 8x + 6$ is $f(x) = 2(x - 2)^2 + 1$. This tells us that the vertex of the parabola is at $(2, 1)$.

### Subsection: Completing the square with complex numbers

In some cases, completing the square will result in a negative number under the square root, which leads to complex solutions. Complex numbers are numbers that include the square root of a negative number, typically denoted by the letter $i$.

Let's look at an example.

#### Example: Solve $x^2 + 4x + 5 = 0$ by completing the square

1. **Make sure the coefficient of $x^2$ is 1:** In this case, the coefficient of $x^2$ is already 1, so we can move to the next step.

2. **Move the constant term to the right side of the equation:** Subtract 5 from both sides to get $x^2 + 4x = -5$.

3. **Complete the square on the left side of the equation and balance this by adding the same value to the right side of the equation:** Half of the coefficient of $x$ is 2, and squaring this gives 4. Add 4 to both sides to get $x^2 + 4x + 4 = -1$.

4. **Factor the left side of the equation:** The left side factors to $(x + 2)^2$.

5. **Write the equation in vertex form:** We now have $(x + 2)^2 = -1$.

6. **Solve for $x$:** Taking the square root of both sides gives $x + 2 = \pm i$, so $x = -2 \pm i$.

So, the solutions to the equation $x^2 + 4x + 5 = 0$ are $x = -2 + i$ and $x = -2 - i$.

As you can see, completing the square is a versatile method that can be used to solve a wide range of quadratic equations, even those that involve complex numbers. By mastering this technique, you will be well-equipped to tackle any quadratic equation that comes your way.

### Section: More on completing the square

#### Subsection: Using completing the square to solve real-world problems

Completing the square is not just a mathematical technique for solving equations or rewriting functions. It can also be used to solve real-world problems. Let's look at an example.

#### Example: A farmer wants to build a rectangular pen for his sheep. He has 100 meters of fencing and wants to maximize the area of the pen. What should the dimensions of the pen be?

1. **Formulate the problem as a quadratic function:** The area of a rectangle is given by the product of its length and width. If we let $x$ represent the width of the pen, then the length is $100 - 2x$ (since the farmer has 100 meters of fencing and each side of the rectangle requires fencing). Therefore, the area $A$ of the pen can be represented by the function $A(x) = x(100 - 2x) = 100x - 2x^2$.

2. **Rewrite the function in vertex form by completing the square:** To find the maximum area, we need to find the vertex of the parabola represented by the function $A(x)$. We can do this by completing the square. First, we rewrite the function as $-2x^2 + 100x$. Then, we factor out a -2 to get $-2(x^2 - 50x)$. Next, we complete the square inside the parentheses. Half of -50 is -25, and squaring this gives 625. So, we add and subtract 625 inside the parentheses to get $-2[(x - 25)^2 - 625]$. Finally, we simplify this to get $A(x) = -2(x - 25)^2 + 1250$.

3. **Find the vertex of the parabola:** The vertex form of a parabola is $f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$, where $(h, k)$ is the vertex of the parabola. So, the vertex of the parabola represented by $A(x)$ is $(25, 1250)$.

4. **Interpret the result in the context of the problem:** The $x$-coordinate of the vertex represents the width of the pen that maximizes the area, and the $y$-coordinate represents the maximum area. So, the farmer should make the width of the pen 25 meters to maximize the area, which will be 1250 square meters.

This example illustrates how completing the square can be used to solve real-world problems. By rewriting a quadratic function in vertex form, we can easily find the maximum or minimum value of the function and the value of $x$ that gives this maximum or minimum. This is a powerful tool for solving a wide range of problems.

### Section: More on completing the square

#### Subsection: Comparing methods for solving quadratic equations

In the previous sections, we have learned several methods for solving quadratic equations, including factoring, using the quadratic formula, and completing the square. Each method has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the best method to use often depends on the specific problem at hand. Let's compare these methods.

1. **Factoring:** Factoring is a straightforward method that can be used when the quadratic equation can be easily factored. For example, the equation $x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$ can be factored to $(x - 2)(x - 3) = 0$, from which we can find the solutions $x = 2$ and $x = 3$. However, not all quadratic equations can be easily factored, especially when the coefficients are not integers or when the equation has no rational roots.

2. **Quadratic formula:** The quadratic formula, $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$, can be used to solve any quadratic equation, regardless of the coefficients. It is a powerful tool, but it can be cumbersome to use, especially when the coefficients are large or when the discriminant $b^2 - 4ac$ is not a perfect square.

3. **Completing the square:** Completing the square is a method that can be used to rewrite a quadratic equation in vertex form, $f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$, which can make it easier to solve. This method is particularly useful when the quadratic equation represents a real-world problem, as it allows us to find the maximum or minimum value of the function and the value of $x$ that gives this maximum or minimum. However, completing the square involves several steps and can be more time-consuming than the other methods.

In conclusion, while each method has its own strengths and weaknesses, they all provide valuable tools for solving quadratic equations. The key is to understand how each method works and to choose the most appropriate method for the problem at hand. Practice using each method to become proficient in solving quadratic equations.

### Section: Strategizing to solve quadratic equations

#### Subsection: Choosing the best method to solve a quadratic equation

When faced with a quadratic equation, it's important to have a strategy for choosing the best method to solve it. Here are some tips to help you decide:

1. **Check if the equation can be easily factored:** If the quadratic equation can be factored easily, then factoring is the quickest and simplest method to use. For example, if you're given the equation $x^2 - 3x - 10 = 0$, you can quickly see that it factors to $(x - 5)(x + 2) = 0$, and the solutions are $x = 5$ and $x = -2$.

2. **Consider the quadratic formula for complex or irrational solutions:** If the coefficients of the quadratic equation are large or if you suspect that the solutions are complex or irrational, the quadratic formula is a good choice. For example, the equation $x^2 - 2x + 2 = 0$ has complex solutions, which can be found using the quadratic formula.

3. **Use completing the square for real-world problems:** If the quadratic equation represents a real-world problem where you need to find the maximum or minimum value of a function, completing the square is the best method to use. This method allows you to rewrite the equation in vertex form, $f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$, which makes it easy to find the vertex of the parabola and hence the maximum or minimum value.

4. **Consider the quadratic formula if the equation is hard to factor:** If the quadratic equation is hard to factor, the quadratic formula is a reliable method that can be used to find the solutions. For example, the equation $x^2 - 7x + 1 = 0$ is hard to factor, but the solutions can be found easily using the quadratic formula.

Remember, the key to mastering quadratic equations is practice. The more you practice, the better you'll get at recognizing which method is best for a given problem. And don't forget, it's always a good idea to check your solutions by substituting them back into the original equation to make sure they work.

### Section: Strategizing to solve quadratic equations

#### Subsection: Solving complex quadratic equations

Complex quadratic equations are those that have complex solutions. These solutions are not real numbers, but rather, they are numbers that involve the imaginary unit $i$, where $i^2 = -1$. 

For example, consider the quadratic equation $x^2 + 1 = 0$. This equation cannot be solved using real numbers because there is no real number that, when squared, gives a negative result. However, if we introduce the imaginary unit $i$, we can find the solutions $x = i$ and $x = -i$.

To solve a complex quadratic equation, we can use the quadratic formula:

$$x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$$

The term under the square root, $b^2 - 4ac$, is called the discriminant. If the discriminant is negative, the quadratic equation has complex solutions. In this case, we can rewrite the square root of a negative number as a multiple of $i$.

For example, let's solve the equation $x^2 + 2x + 2 = 0$ using the quadratic formula. The coefficients are $a = 1$, $b = 2$, and $c = 2$, so the discriminant is $b^2 - 4ac = 2^2 - 4*1*2 = -4$. Since the discriminant is negative, we know that the solutions will be complex.

We can rewrite the square root of $-4$ as $2i$, so the solutions are:

$$x = \frac{-2 \pm 2i}{2} = -1 \pm i$$

So, the solutions to the equation $x^2 + 2x + 2 = 0$ are $x = -1 + i$ and $x = -1 - i$.

Remember, complex solutions always come in conjugate pairs, meaning if $a + bi$ is a solution, then $a - bi$ is also a solution. This is a consequence of the $\pm$ in the quadratic formula.

Solving complex quadratic equations may seem daunting at first, but with practice, it becomes just as straightforward as solving real quadratic equations. The key is to remember the quadratic formula and the definition of the imaginary unit $i$.

### Section: Strategizing to solve quadratic equations

#### Subsection: Reviewing strategies for solving quadratic equations

In the previous section, we discussed how to solve complex quadratic equations using the quadratic formula. However, not all quadratic equations will have complex solutions. In fact, the nature of the solutions of a quadratic equation can be determined by the discriminant, $b^2 - 4ac$. 

If the discriminant is positive, the quadratic equation has two distinct real solutions. If the discriminant is zero, the quadratic equation has exactly one real solution (or a repeated real solution). If the discriminant is negative, as we have seen, the quadratic equation has two complex solutions.

Let's review the strategies for solving quadratic equations based on the nature of their solutions:

1. **Factoring:** This is the simplest method for solving quadratic equations, but it only works when the equation can be factored. For example, the equation $x^2 - 5x + 6 = 0$ can be factored as $(x - 2)(x - 3) = 0$, so the solutions are $x = 2$ and $x = 3$.

2. **Completing the square:** This method involves rearranging the equation to a perfect square on one side. For example, the equation $x^2 + 4x - 5 = 0$ can be rearranged as $(x + 2)^2 = 9$, so the solutions are $x = -2 \pm 3$.

3. **Quadratic formula:** As we have seen, this method works for all quadratic equations, whether their solutions are real or complex. The quadratic formula is derived from the process of completing the square.

4. **Graphing:** This method involves plotting the quadratic function $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ and finding the x-intercepts. The x-intercepts of the graph are the solutions of the equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$. This method is useful for visualizing the solutions, but it may not be as precise as the other methods.

Remember, the best strategy for solving a quadratic equation depends on the specific equation and the nature of its solutions. It's important to be familiar with all of these strategies and to practice using them on a variety of problems. With practice, you'll become more comfortable with choosing the best strategy for each problem and executing it effectively.

### Section: Quadratic standard form

The standard form of a quadratic equation is a fundamental concept in algebra. It provides a way to express any quadratic equation in a standardized way, making it easier to analyze and solve. 

The standard form of a quadratic equation is given by:

$$
ax^2 + bx + c = 0
$$

where $a$, $b$, and $c$ are constants, and $a \neq 0$. The variable $x$ is the unknown that we are often trying to solve for. 

#### Subsection: Understanding standard form

Let's break down the components of the standard form:

- $ax^2$: This is the quadratic term. The coefficient $a$ determines the direction and the width of the parabola when the quadratic equation is graphed. If $a$ is positive, the parabola opens upwards, and if $a$ is negative, the parabola opens downwards. The absolute value of $a$ affects the width of the parabola; a larger absolute value of $a$ results in a narrower parabola.

- $bx$: This is the linear term. The coefficient $b$ affects the position of the vertex of the parabola on the x-axis.

- $c$: This is the constant term. It affects the position of the vertex of the parabola on the y-axis.

The standard form of a quadratic equation is very useful because it allows us to easily identify the coefficients $a$, $b$, and $c$, which we can then use in the quadratic formula to find the solutions of the equation. 

In the next section, we will discuss how to convert a quadratic equation to standard form if it is not already in that form.

### Section: Writing quadratic functions in standard form

In the previous section, we discussed the standard form of a quadratic equation and its components. Now, we will learn how to write a quadratic function in standard form.

#### Subsection: Converting to standard form

A quadratic function may not always be given in standard form. It could be in vertex form, which is given by:

$$
y = a(x - h)^2 + k
$$

where $h$ and $k$ are the coordinates of the vertex of the parabola. 

To convert a quadratic function from vertex form to standard form, we need to expand the square in the vertex form equation. Here's how:

1. Start with the vertex form of the equation: $y = a(x - h)^2 + k$.
2. Expand the square: $y = a(x^2 - 2hx + h^2) + k$.
3. Distribute the $a$: $y = ax^2 - 2ahx + ah^2 + k$.
4. Finally, we can rewrite this in the form of the standard equation by identifying $a$, $b$, and $c$: $y = ax^2 + bx + c$, where $b = -2ah$ and $c = ah^2 + k$.

Let's take an example:

Suppose we have a quadratic function in vertex form: $y = 2(x - 3)^2 + 4$.

Following the steps above:

1. Start with the vertex form of the equation: $y = 2(x - 3)^2 + 4$.
2. Expand the square: $y = 2(x^2 - 6x + 9) + 4$.
3. Distribute the $2$: $y = 2x^2 - 12x + 18 + 4$.
4. Combine like terms: $y = 2x^2 - 12x + 22$.

So, the standard form of the given quadratic function is $y = 2x^2 - 12x + 22$.

In the next section, we will discuss how to solve quadratic equations that are in standard form.

### Section: Quadratic standard form

In the previous section, we learned how to convert a quadratic function from vertex form to standard form. Now, we will delve deeper into the standard form of a quadratic function and how to graph it.

The standard form of a quadratic function is given by:

$$
y = ax^2 + bx + c
$$

where $a$, $b$, and $c$ are constants, and $a$ is not equal to zero. The graph of a quadratic function is a parabola. The shape of the parabola depends on the value of $a$. If $a$ is positive, the parabola opens upwards, and if $a$ is negative, the parabola opens downwards.

### Subsection: Graphing quadratic functions in standard form

To graph a quadratic function in standard form, we need to identify a few key features: the vertex, the axis of symmetry, and the y-intercept.

1. **Vertex**: The vertex of the parabola is the point $(h, k)$, where $h = -\frac{b}{2a}$ and $k = c - \frac{b^2}{4a}$. This point is either the highest or lowest point on the graph, depending on whether the parabola opens upwards or downwards.

2. **Axis of Symmetry**: The axis of symmetry is the vertical line $x = h$, which passes through the vertex. The parabola is symmetric about this line.

3. **Y-intercept**: The y-intercept is the point where the graph crosses the y-axis. This point is given by $(0, c)$.

Let's take an example:

Suppose we have a quadratic function in standard form: $y = 2x^2 - 12x + 22$.

1. The vertex is given by $h = -\frac{b}{2a} = -\frac{-12}{2*2} = 3$ and $k = c - \frac{b^2}{4a} = 22 - \frac{(-12)^2}{4*2} = 4$. So, the vertex is $(3, 4)$.

2. The axis of symmetry is the line $x = 3$.

3. The y-intercept is $(0, 22)$.

Now, we can plot these points and draw a smooth curve to complete the graph of the quadratic function.

In the next section, we will discuss how to solve quadratic equations that are in standard form.

### Section: Features & forms of quadratic functions

Quadratic functions are not only defined by their standard form, but also by their key features. These features include the vertex, the axis of symmetry, and the y-intercept, which we have already discussed in the previous section. In addition to these, there are other important features such as the direction of opening, the maximum or minimum value, and the x-intercepts or roots.

#### Subsection: Identifying key features of quadratic functions

1. **Direction of Opening**: As we have already learned, the direction in which the parabola opens depends on the value of $a$. If $a$ is positive, the parabola opens upwards, indicating that the function has a minimum value. If $a$ is negative, the parabola opens downwards, indicating that the function has a maximum value.

2. **Maximum or Minimum Value**: The maximum or minimum value of a quadratic function is the y-coordinate of the vertex. If the parabola opens upwards, the minimum value is $k$, and if it opens downwards, the maximum value is $k$.

3. **X-intercepts or Roots**: The x-intercepts of a quadratic function are the points where the graph crosses the x-axis. These points are also known as the roots or zeros of the function. They can be found by setting $y = 0$ in the standard form equation and solving for $x$. This gives us the quadratic equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$.

Let's take an example:

Suppose we have a quadratic function in standard form: $y = -x^2 + 6x - 8$.

1. The direction of opening is downwards because $a = -1$ which is less than zero. So, the function has a maximum value.

2. The vertex is given by $h = -\frac{b}{2a} = -\frac{6}{2*(-1)} = -3$ and $k = c - \frac{b^2}{4a} = -8 - \frac{6^2}{4*(-1)} = 1$. So, the vertex is $(-3, 1)$ and the maximum value is $1$.

3. The x-intercepts can be found by setting $y = 0$ and solving for $x$. This gives us the quadratic equation $-x^2 + 6x - 8 = 0$. Solving this equation gives us the roots $x = 2$ and $x = 4$.

In the next section, we will discuss the different forms of quadratic functions and how to convert between them.

#### Subsection: Comparing forms of quadratic functions

Quadratic functions can be represented in three main forms: standard form, vertex form, and factored form. Each form provides different insights into the function's properties and behavior.

1. **Standard Form**: The standard form of a quadratic function is $y = ax^2 + bx + c$. This form is most useful for finding the y-intercept of the function, which is simply the constant term $c$. It also allows us to derive the quadratic formula for finding the roots of the function.

2. **Vertex Form**: The vertex form of a quadratic function is $y = a(x-h)^2 + k$. This form is particularly useful for identifying the vertex of the parabola, which is given by the point $(h, k)$. It also clearly shows whether the parabola opens upwards or downwards based on the sign of $a$.

3. **Factored Form**: The factored form of a quadratic function is $y = a(x - r)(x - s)$. This form is most useful for finding the x-intercepts or roots of the function, which are given by the points $r$ and $s$.

Let's compare these forms using the same quadratic function from the previous example: $y = -x^2 + 6x - 8$.

1. In standard form, we can easily see that the y-intercept is $-8$.

2. To convert this to vertex form, we complete the square: $y = -(x^2 - 6x) - 8 = -(x^2 - 6x + 9) + 1 = -(x - 3)^2 + 1$. Now, we can clearly see that the vertex is $(3, 1)$ and the parabola opens downwards.

3. To convert this to factored form, we factor the quadratic expression: $y = -(x^2 - 6x + 8) = -(x - 2)(x - 4)$. Now, we can easily see that the x-intercepts are $2$ and $4$.

As you can see, each form of the quadratic function provides different insights. Understanding these forms and how to convert between them is a key part of mastering quadratic functions and equations.

### Section: Features & forms of quadratic functions

Quadratic functions are not just abstract mathematical concepts. They have practical applications in various real-world scenarios. Understanding the features and forms of quadratic functions can help us solve problems in fields like physics, economics, engineering, and more.

#### Subsection: Using features and forms to solve real-world problems

Let's consider a real-world example to illustrate how the different forms of quadratic functions can be used to solve problems.

**Example**: A ball is thrown upwards from a height of 1 meter with an initial velocity of 5 meters per second. The height of the ball after $t$ seconds is given by the equation $h = -4.9t^2 + 5t + 1$. 

This equation is a quadratic function in standard form, where $h$ represents the height of the ball, and $t$ represents time. The coefficient of $t^2$ is negative, indicating that the parabola opens downwards, which makes sense as the ball will eventually fall back to the ground due to gravity.

1. **Standard Form**: From the standard form, we can easily find the initial height of the ball, which is the y-intercept $c = 1$ meter. 

2. **Vertex Form**: To find the maximum height reached by the ball and the time it takes to reach that height, we can convert the equation to vertex form. Completing the square, we get $h = -4.9(t^2 - \frac{5}{4.9}t) + 1 = -4.9[(t - \frac{5}{2*4.9})^2 - (\frac{5}{2*4.9})^2] + 1 = -4.9(t - 0.51)^2 + 2.55$. The vertex is $(0.51, 2.55)$, so the ball reaches its maximum height of 2.55 meters after 0.51 seconds.

3. **Factored Form**: To find when the ball hits the ground, we can convert the equation to factored form to find the roots. Factoring the equation, we get $h = -4.9(t - 0)(t - 1.02)$. The roots are $0$ and $1.02$, so the ball hits the ground after 1.02 seconds.

As you can see, each form of the quadratic function provides different insights that help us understand and solve the problem. Mastering these forms and knowing when to use each one is a crucial part of algebra.

### Section: Comparing quadratic functions

In the previous section, we learned about the different forms of quadratic functions and how they can be used to solve real-world problems. Now, let's move on to comparing quadratic functions. This skill is essential in situations where we have more than one quadratic function and we need to determine which one best fits our needs or provides the most accurate solution.

#### Subsection: Comparing graphs of quadratic functions

One of the most effective ways to compare quadratic functions is by graphing them. The graph of a quadratic function is a curve called a parabola. By comparing the graphs of different quadratic functions, we can gain insights into their properties and how they relate to each other.

Let's consider two quadratic functions:

1. $f(x) = x^2 - 2x + 1$
2. $g(x) = -x^2 + 4x - 3$

Both functions are in standard form. The first function, $f(x)$, opens upwards because the coefficient of $x^2$ is positive. The second function, $g(x)$, opens downwards because the coefficient of $x^2$ is negative.

**Comparing the vertices**: The vertex of a parabola is the point where it reaches its maximum or minimum value. For $f(x)$, the vertex is at $(1, 0)$, and for $g(x)$, the vertex is at $(2, 1)$. This tells us that $f(x)$ reaches its minimum value at $x = 1$, while $g(x)$ reaches its maximum value at $x = 2$.

**Comparing the y-intercepts**: The y-intercept of a function is the point where the graph intersects the y-axis. For $f(x)$, the y-intercept is at $(0, 1)$, and for $g(x)$, the y-intercept is at $(0, -3)$. This tells us that $f(x)$ starts at a higher point on the y-axis than $g(x)$.

**Comparing the roots**: The roots of a function are the values of $x$ for which the function equals zero. For $f(x)$, the roots are $x = 1$, and for $g(x)$, the roots are $x = 1$ and $x = 3$. This tells us that $f(x)$ intersects the x-axis at one point, while $g(x)$ intersects the x-axis at two points.

By comparing the graphs of these two functions, we can see that they have different shapes, vertices, y-intercepts, and roots. This comparison can help us understand how changes in the coefficients of a quadratic function affect its graph and properties. 

In the next section, we will learn how to solve quadratic equations, which is a crucial skill in algebra.

#### Subsection: Comparing equations of quadratic functions

In addition to comparing the graphs of quadratic functions, we can also compare their equations. This method is particularly useful when we don't have the ability to graph the functions or when we want to make precise comparisons.

Let's consider the same two quadratic functions from the previous subsection:

1. $f(x) = x^2 - 2x + 1$
2. $g(x) = -x^2 + 4x - 3$

**Comparing the coefficients**: The coefficients of the quadratic terms ($x^2$), linear terms ($x$), and constant terms can tell us a lot about the functions. For $f(x)$, the coefficients are 1, -2, and 1, respectively. For $g(x)$, the coefficients are -1, 4, and -3, respectively. The negative coefficient of $x^2$ in $g(x)$ indicates that the parabola opens downwards, as we saw in the previous subsection.

**Comparing the discriminants**: The discriminant of a quadratic function, given by the formula $b^2 - 4ac$, can tell us about the number and type of roots the function has. For $f(x)$, the discriminant is $(-2)^2 - 4(1)(1) = 0$, indicating that it has one real root. For $g(x)$, the discriminant is $(4)^2 - 4(-1)(-3) = 4$, indicating that it has two distinct real roots.

**Comparing the axis of symmetry**: The axis of symmetry of a quadratic function, given by the formula $x = -b/2a$, can tell us where the vertex of the parabola is. For $f(x)$, the axis of symmetry is $x = -(-2)/2(1) = 1$. For $g(x)$, the axis of symmetry is $x = -(4)/2(-1) = 2$. This confirms what we found by comparing the vertices in the previous subsection.

By comparing the equations of quadratic functions, we can gain a deeper understanding of their properties and how they relate to each other. This method is especially useful when we need to make precise comparisons or when graphing is not feasible.

#### Subsection: Using comparisons to solve problems

Now that we have learned how to compare quadratic functions, let's apply this knowledge to solve problems. This will help us understand the practical applications of these comparisons.

**Example 1**: Let's consider two quadratic functions:

1. $h(x) = 2x^2 - 3x + 1$
2. $j(x) = -x^2 + 5x - 2$

We are asked to find which function has a larger y-intercept. The y-intercept of a function is the value of the function when $x = 0$. So, we substitute $x = 0$ into both functions:

$h(0) = 2(0)^2 - 3(0) + 1 = 1$

$j(0) = -(0)^2 + 5(0) - 2 = -2$

Therefore, $h(x)$ has a larger y-intercept.

**Example 2**: Let's consider the same two functions and find which one has a larger vertex. The vertex of a quadratic function is given by the point $(-b/2a, f(-b/2a))$. So, we calculate the vertex for both functions:

For $h(x)$, the vertex is $(-(-3)/2(2), h(-(-3)/2(2))) = (0.75, 0.875)$.

For $j(x)$, the vertex is $(-(5)/2(-1), j(-(5)/2(-1))) = (2.5, 4.75)$.

Therefore, $j(x)$ has a larger vertex.

By comparing different aspects of quadratic functions, we can solve a variety of problems. This method is particularly useful when we need to make precise comparisons or when graphing is not feasible. As we continue to study quadratic functions, we will find that these comparisons become increasingly important in understanding and solving complex problems.

### Section: Transforming quadratic functions

In the previous section, we learned how to compare different aspects of quadratic functions. Now, we will explore how to transform these functions. Transformations can help us understand how changes in the coefficients of a quadratic function affect its graph.

#### Subsection: Understanding transformations of quadratic functions

A quadratic function is generally expressed in the form $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$. The graph of this function is a parabola. The transformations of a quadratic function can be categorized into three types: vertical shifts, horizontal shifts, and reflections.

**Vertical Shifts**

A vertical shift occurs when a constant is added or subtracted to the function. If a constant $k$ is added to the function $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$, the graph of the function shifts upward by $k$ units. If $k$ is subtracted, the graph shifts downward by $k$ units. 

For example, if we have a function $f(x) = x^2$ and we add 3 to it, the new function $g(x) = x^2 + 3$ will be a vertical shift of the original function $f(x)$ upward by 3 units.

**Horizontal Shifts**

A horizontal shift occurs when a constant is added or subtracted to the $x$ in the function. If a constant $h$ is subtracted from $x$ in the function $f(x) = a(x-h)^2 + k$, the graph of the function shifts to the right by $h$ units. If $h$ is added, the graph shifts to the left by $h$ units.

For example, if we have a function $f(x) = x^2$ and we subtract 2 from $x$, the new function $g(x) = (x-2)^2$ will be a horizontal shift of the original function $f(x)$ to the right by 2 units.

**Reflections**

A reflection occurs when the sign of $a$ in the function $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$ is changed. If $a$ is positive, the graph opens upward, and if $a$ is negative, the graph opens downward.

For example, if we have a function $f(x) = x^2$ and we change the sign of $x^2$, the new function $g(x) = -x^2$ will be a reflection of the original function $f(x)$ over the x-axis.

Understanding these transformations is crucial for graphing quadratic functions and solving complex problems. In the next section, we will learn how to apply these transformations to solve problems.

#### Subsection: Graphing transformations of quadratic functions

Now that we understand the types of transformations that can occur with quadratic functions, let's learn how to graph these transformations. 

**Graphing Vertical Shifts**

To graph a vertical shift, we start by graphing the original function $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$. Then, we shift every point on the graph up or down by $k$ units, depending on whether $k$ was added or subtracted to the function.

For example, to graph the function $g(x) = x^2 + 3$, we start by graphing $f(x) = x^2$. Then, we shift every point on the graph of $f(x)$ upward by 3 units to get the graph of $g(x)$.

**Graphing Horizontal Shifts**

To graph a horizontal shift, we again start by graphing the original function $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$. Then, we shift every point on the graph left or right by $h$ units, depending on whether $h$ was subtracted or added to $x$ in the function.

For example, to graph the function $g(x) = (x-2)^2$, we start by graphing $f(x) = x^2$. Then, we shift every point on the graph of $f(x)$ to the right by 2 units to get the graph of $g(x)$.

**Graphing Reflections**

To graph a reflection, we start by graphing the original function $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$. If $a$ is negative, we reflect every point on the graph over the x-axis to get the graph of the reflected function.

For example, to graph the function $g(x) = -x^2$, we start by graphing $f(x) = x^2$. Then, we reflect every point on the graph of $f(x)$ over the x-axis to get the graph of $g(x)$.

Remember, when graphing transformations of quadratic functions, it's always helpful to start by graphing the original function and then applying the transformation. This will give you a clear visual understanding of how the transformation affects the graph of the function.

#### Subsection: Writing equations for transformed quadratic functions

After understanding how to graph transformations of quadratic functions, let's now learn how to write equations for these transformed functions. 

**Writing Equations for Vertical Shifts**

A vertical shift of a quadratic function can be represented by the equation $g(x) = f(x) + k$, where $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$ is the original function and $k$ is the number of units the function is shifted up (if $k$ is positive) or down (if $k$ is negative).

For example, if we have the function $f(x) = x^2$ and we want to shift it up by 3 units, the equation of the transformed function would be $g(x) = x^2 + 3$.

**Writing Equations for Horizontal Shifts**

A horizontal shift of a quadratic function can be represented by the equation $g(x) = f(x-h)$, where $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$ is the original function and $h$ is the number of units the function is shifted to the right (if $h$ is positive) or to the left (if $h$ is negative).

For example, if we have the function $f(x) = x^2$ and we want to shift it to the right by 2 units, the equation of the transformed function would be $g(x) = (x-2)^2$.

**Writing Equations for Reflections**

A reflection of a quadratic function over the x-axis can be represented by the equation $g(x) = -f(x)$, where $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$ is the original function.

For example, if we have the function $f(x) = x^2$ and we want to reflect it over the x-axis, the equation of the transformed function would be $g(x) = -x^2$.

Remember, when writing equations for transformations of quadratic functions, it's always helpful to start by identifying the original function and then applying the transformation. This will give you a clear understanding of how the transformation affects the equation of the function.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of quadratic functions and equations, a fundamental topic in Algebra 1. We have explored the standard form of a quadratic equation, $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$, and learned how to solve it using various methods such as factoring, completing the square, and the quadratic formula. 

We have also examined the properties of quadratic functions, including their parabolic shape, axis of symmetry, and vertex. We have seen how these properties can be used to graph quadratic functions and to solve real-world problems. 

Furthermore, we have learned how to convert a quadratic function from standard form to vertex form, and vice versa. This skill is crucial for understanding the relationship between the coefficients of a quadratic function and its graph.

In summary, mastering quadratic functions and equations is a critical step in your journey through Algebra 1. The concepts and skills you have learned in this chapter will serve as a solid foundation for your future studies in mathematics.
